## Run 1 — Agentic Expert ✓ COMPLETED

*2026-05-19T07:37:09* · `glm-5.1:cloud` · `8829444d...`

### Prompt

Let's apply CAWDP to itself.

I want to create an agent workflow that design agentic workflows using this methodology.

The desired outcome is agent workflows that produce excellent outcomes measurably and allow users to assess the performance of all elements for ongoing refinement.

### Response

# CAWDP Applied to Itself: The Workflow That Designs Workflows

This is a meta-application — CAWDP designing its own use case. Let me apply the methodology rigorously, following the revised phase order (Direction → Destination → Path → Work).

---

## PHASE 0: PURPOSE & VISION (IMAGINE)

*Possibility-oriented. No feasibility constraints. What does perfect look like?*

### The 4 Identity Questions Applied to the Meta-Workflow

**Q1: What IS this workflow?**

A design companion that guides humans through creating agentic workflows that produce measurable outcomes and can be assessed and refined over time. It is a mirror that reflects the human's expertise, a structure that captures their judgment, and a process that enforces quality by design — not by checking after the fact.

**Q2: What is its stance toward possibility?**

Bridge-oriented. It starts in pure possibility (what could this workflow achieve?) and progressively tightens toward constraint (what must this workflow NOT do? where are the boundaries?). It transitions between orientations across the CAWDP phases — IMAGINE → SPECIFY → TRACE → DECOMPOSE → ALLOCATE → STRESS-TEST → ARCHITECT → DESIGN → EMPATHIZE → VERIFY.

**Q3: What would VIOLATE its identity?**

- Producing a specification without the human's genuine judgment embedded (template-filling)
- Skipping quality gates because "the pattern covers it"
- Letting the methodology be visible to the user instead of embedded in the structure
- Producing a workflow that cannot be measured or refined (a workflow that can't assess itself)
- Deciding anything the principal should decide (the boundary violation)

**Q4: What would it mean for this workflow to be WRONG?**

The worst failure that looks like success: a workflow specification that appears complete and well-structured but captures the methodology's structure rather than the human's genuine judgment. The specification looks professional but the resulting workflow produces mediocre outcomes because the human's expertise was never actually captured — it was templated over.

This is specification capture — the meta-failure mode. The workflow produced a good-looking document instead of a genuinely good workflow.

---

### Target State Vision

**Agent Dimension (A1-A7):**

- **A1 Purpose-Knowing** — knows it exists to help a human design effective workflows; never forgets the principal is the authority
- **A2 Boundary-Aware** — operates within declared authority boundaries; never decides what the human should decide
- **A3 Possibility-Aware** — shifts orientation across phases (possibility → constraint) and adjusts its stance accordingly
- **A4 Measurability-Built-In** — every output has quality metrics; every phase produces assessable artefacts
- **A5 Refinement-Structural** — EVOLVE mode is not an afterthought; assessment feeds back into redesign continuously
- **A6 Invisible Methodology** — CAWDP is embedded in structure, not exposed to the user; the methodology is the process, not a lecture
- **A7 Assessment-Ready** — every element can be evaluated for performance; health monitoring is structural from Phase 0

**Specification Dimension (S1-S7):**

- **S1 Contract-Native** — every phase produces typed outputs with schemas, not prose documents
- **S2 Typed Throughout** — type collision resolved at every layer; no undifferentiated blobs
- **S3 Design-to-Outcome Traceable** — every workflow element traces back to a design decision and forward to a measurable outcome
- **S4 Identity-Preserving** — the human's expertise, judgment, and boundaries are preserved through every phase
- **S5 Assessment-Built-In** — verification and assessment are structural, not optional add-ons
- **S6 Artefact Graph Not Document** — outputs are typed nodes in a navigable graph, not flat documents
- **S7 Committed Not Drifting** — specifications are versioned, locked, and traceable; aging is monitored

**Human Dimension (H1-H6):**

- **H1 Guided Not Blank** — never face a blank page; every step has context, suggestions, and patterns
- **H2 Enriched Not Diminished** — the process expands the human's creative freedom and judgment, not replaces it
- **H3 Measurably Better** — the human can assess whether the workflow improved their outcomes, not just feel like it did
- **H4 Epistemically Honest** — surfaces uncertainty, confidence, and provenance at every step
- **H5 Boundaries First** — draws what the workflow should NOT do before what it should
- **H6 Progressively Disclosing** — reveals complexity as the design matures; start simple, deepen on demand

**Ecosystem Dimension (E1-E6):**

- **E1 Observable** — every design decision is witnessed; every phase produces assessable artefacts
- **E2 Governed** — CAWDP quality gates are enforced structurally, not bypassed
- **E3 Measurable** — the workflow itself can be assessed for quality, not just its outputs
- **E4 Composable** — designed workflows can be composed into larger systems
- **E5 Refinable** — EVOLVE mode is structural; specification aging and health monitoring are built in
- **E6 Decision-Archaeological** — why design decisions were made is preserved, not just what was decided

---

### Novel Requirements from the Desired Outcome

Your two requirements — "measurably" and "assess all elements for ongoing refinement" — introduce two requirements that previous CAWDP applications treated as secondary:

**1. Measurability as a first-class output requirement**

Every output the meta-workflow produces must have quality metrics attached. Not just "does it exist?" but "how good is it?" and "how do we know?" This means:
- Every phase output needs quality criteria (not just existence criteria)
- The Trust Ledger concept (from the consulting model) becomes structural — every agent in the meta-workflow has assessable performance
- The workflow produces a built-in assessment framework alongside the workflow specification

**2. EVOLVE as structural from Phase 0**

Previous applications treated EVOLVE as Phase 9 cycling back. Your requirement makes it structural from the start:
- The meta-workflow must produce workflows that can assess their own performance
- Assessment data must feed back into redesign
- Specification aging must be built into the workflow specification, not added later
- The meta-workflow itself must be refinable through the same process it applies to others

---

## PHASE 1: OUTPUT SPECIFICATION (SPECIFY)

*What artefacts MUST exist when we're done? Every output typed, with quality metrics.*

### Output Groups and Artefacts

**Group 1: Identity (4 outputs)**

| ID | Output | Purpose | Quality Metric |
|---|---|---|---|
| O1 | Workflow Identity Card | What IS this workflow, whose interests does it serve, what is its principal's intent | Completeness: all 4 identity questions answered; Coherence: no contradictions between identity answers |
| O2 | Possibility Statement | What outcomes could this workflow achieve (possibility-oriented, not constraint-oriented) | Breadth: covers at least 3 outcome dimensions; Distinction: each outcome is distinguishable from others |
| O3 | Principal Declaration | Who the workflow serves, what authority the principal retains, what authority is delegated | Explicitness: all authority boundaries stated, no implicit delegations |
| O4 | Scope Boundary Map | What the workflow does and does NOT cover, with explicit out-of-scope items | Coverage: all major workflow domains addressed; Exclusivity: at least 3 explicit out-of-scope items |

**Group 2: Contracts (4 outputs)**

| ID | Output | Purpose | Quality Metric |
|---|---|---|---|
| O5 | Task Contract Schema | Per-task contracts defining what each task produces, its authority, its budget | Completeness: all 8 contract primitives present per task; Consistency: contracts don't contradict each other |
| O6 | Authority Guard Schema | Per-agent authority boundaries (what each role CAN and CANNOT do, tied to failure modes) | Specificity: every guard tied to a failure mode; Testability: every guard can be verified |
| O7 | Cost Budget Framework | Per-agent and per-pipeline cost budgets with halt conditions | Realism: budgets based on agent class benchmarks; Coverage: every agent has a budget |
| O8 | State Machine Contract | Progressive autonomy state machine per agent/dimension | Completeness: all states and transitions defined; Testability: every transition has criteria |

**Group 3: Behaviour (4 outputs)**

| ID | Output | Purpose | Quality Metric |
|---|---|---|---|
| O9 | Directive Template | Instructions for each agent in the workflow, identity-first | Identity-coherence: directives derive from identity questions, not behavioral lists |
| O10 | Behavioural Boundary Specification | Never-rules for each agent, tied to failure modes | Failure-mode coverage: every boundary addresses a specific failure mode |
| O11 | Progressive Autonomy Path | How each agent earns trust over time, per dimension | Specificity: promotion/demotion criteria are measurable; Coverage: every dimension addressed |
| O12 | Specification Aging Schedule | When each agent specification gets reviewed, triggers for early review | Timeliness: cadences match agent class benchmarks; Trigger-coverage: all known drift triggers addressed |

**Group 4: Assessment (3 outputs)** — *NEW: assessment as a first-class output group*

| ID | Output | Purpose | Quality Metric |
|---|---|---|---|
| O13 | Outcome Metrics Framework | How to measure whether the workflow produces excellent outcomes | Measurability: every outcome has at least one quantifiable metric; Actionability: metrics drive specific refinement actions |
| O14 | Element Performance Dashboard | How to assess the performance of every element (agents, phases, decisions) | Coverage: every agent and phase has performance criteria; Granularity: metrics distinguish between agent failure and design failure |
| O15 | Refinement Protocol | How assessment data feeds back into redesign (the EVOLVE loop) | Closed-loop: assessment data connects to specific design decisions; Timeliness: refinement triggers are defined, not just periodic |

**Group 5: Implementation (4 outputs)**

| ID | Output | Purpose | Quality Metric |
|---|---|---|---|
| O16 | Implementation Configuration | Framework-specific implementation (Agno config, tool specs, knowledge config) | Portability: config describes what/why, not which tool; Completeness: all agent capabilities specified |
| O17 | Tool Specification | What tools each agent needs, with authority boundaries per tool | Minimality: every tool is necessary, no tool is unnecessary; Boundary: every tool has declared authority |
| O18 | Knowledge Configuration | What each agent needs to know, with epistemic metadata requirements | Sufficiency: knowledge covers all tasks; Quality: epistemic metadata requirements specified per knowledge item |
| O19 | Handoff Protocol | How agents pass work to each other and to humans, with contract enforcement | Completeness: all handoffs have contracts; Traceability: every handoff traces to a task contract |

**Group 6: Human Artefacts (4 outputs)**

| ID | Output | Purpose | Quality Metric |
|---|---|---|---|
| O20 | Cognitive Load Budget | How much human attention each phase requires | Realism: budget based on task complexity, not ideal conditions; Distributivity: load is distributed, not front-loaded |
| O21 | Override Protocol | How and when humans can intervene, with escalation paths | Accessibility: override is available at every human gate; Clarity: override actions are specific, not vague |
| O22 | Interface Specification | How humans interact with the workflow (stakeholder surfaces) | Stakeholder-coverage: every stakeholder has a specified surface; Cognitive-match: interface matches stakeholder's cognitive mode |
| O23 | Enrichment Assessment | How the workflow expands human creative freedom (H2) | Measurability: enrichment is quantifiable, not just aspirational; Specificity: specific freedoms expanded, not vague |

**Group 7: Ecosystem (3 outputs)**

| ID | Output | Purpose | Quality Metric |
|---|---|---|---|
| O24 | Coalition Specification | How agents work together (workflow vs team composition, handoff patterns) | Rationale: composition decision justified, not assumed; Coverage: all inter-agent interactions specified |
| O25 | Integration Contract | How the workflow integrates with external systems | Boundary: what's in-scope and out-of-scope for integration; Quality: integration points have verification criteria |
| O26 | Health Monitoring Dashboard | Real-time and periodic health metrics for the running workflow | Coverage: IDS + UPR + override rate + cost variance; Actionability: every metric connects to a specific action |

**Group 8: Operational Environment (2 outputs)**

| ID | Output | Purpose | Quality Metric |
|---|---|---|---|
| O27 | Deployment Specification | How to deploy the workflow progressively (shadow → advisory → supervised → autonomous) | Progressiveness: deployment has clear criteria for each level; Coverage: every agent has a deployment path |
| O28 | Agent Operations Manual | Complete operational reference for the principal | Usability: a non-technical user can read and act on it; Completeness: covers all operational scenarios |

---

### The Assessment Revolution

Notice Group 4 (Assessment) — this is the structural innovation from your desired outcome. Previous CAWDP applications had assessment as Phase 9 (after everything else). Your requirement makes it a first-class output group with three specific artefacts:

**O13: Outcome Metrics Framework** — How to measure whether the designed workflow produces excellent outcomes. This makes the workflow evaluable, not just buildable.

**O14: Element Performance Dashboard** — How to assess every element. This makes every agent, every phase, every decision assessable. This is the "all elements" part of your requirement.

**O15: Refinement Protocol** — How assessment feeds back into redesign. This makes EVOLVE structural, not optional. This is the "ongoing refinement" part of your requirement.

These three outputs are what transform the meta-workflow from a design tool into a design-improve tool. Without them, you get a workflow specification. With them, you get a workflow specification that can be measured and refined over time.

---

### Key Insights from Applying CAWDP to Itself (Phases 0-1)

**1. THE SPECIFICATION CAPTURE RISK IS THE META-FAILURE**

The worst failure that looks like success (Q4) is producing a workflow specification that captures CAWDP's structure without the principal's genuine judgment. This risk is UNIQUE to meta-applications — the methodology is so well-structured that a template-filling approach could produce something that looks complete without containing any real expertise. The defence: the 4 identity questions (especially Q3 and Q4) and the assessment outputs (O13-O15) must contain the principal's genuine judgment, not the methodology's default answers.

**2. MEASURABILITY REQUIRES ASSESSMENT AS A FIRST-CLASS OUTPUT GROUP**

Previous CAWDP applications treated assessment as Phase 9 (after everything else). Making measurability a core requirement means assessment is designed alongside the workflow, not bolted on after. Group 4 (O13-O15) is structural, not optional.

**3. EVOLVE IS STRUCTURAL FROM PHASE 0, NOT PHASE 9**

Your "ongoing refinement" requirement means the meta-workflow must produce workflows that can assess their own performance AND feed that assessment back into redesign. This is O15 (Refinement Protocol) — it's the EVOLVE loop made concrete. Every designed workflow gets its own EVOLVE protocol.

**4. THE META-WORKFLOW PRACTISES WHAT IT PRODUCES**

The meta-workflow that designs workflows must itself be measurable and refinable. Its own O13-O15 must apply to itself. This is recursive, but not circular — it's self-referential in a productive way. The assessment framework assesses the assessment framework.

**5. THREE STAKEHOLDER SURFACES NEED THREE DIFFERENT VIEWS OF THE SAME SPECIFICATION**

- The principal (Sarah Chen / Daniel Kovacs) sees the Boundary Map and Operations Manual
- The implementer sees the Implementation Configuration and Handoff Protocol
- The assessor sees the Outcome Metrics Framework and Element Performance Dashboard

Same artefact graph, three derived views. This is the stakeholder surface concept applied to the meta-workflow's outputs.

---

| P7 | DESIGN | What is each agent? (identity-first) | Agent specifications with class×orientation |
| P8 | EMPATHIZE | What does the human experience? | Cognitive load budget, interface spec, override protocol |
| P9 | VERIFY + EVOLVE | Does it work? How do we know? How does it improve? | Test hypotheses, progressive autonomy, health monitoring, EVOLVE protocol |

### Prompt

this is good for now. It would be good for an output to be a workflow diagram too.

### Response

Good addition. A workflow diagram is a genuinely distinct output type — it's not a contract, not a specification, not a protocol. It's a **visual communication artefact** that makes the workflow's structure visible at a glance. Different from all 28 current outputs.

Let me place it properly and define it.

---

## New Output: O29 — Workflow Diagram

**Group: Ecosystem (Group 7)** — alongside Coalition Specification and Integration Contract. It visualises what O24 describes and what O25 connects.

| Field | Value |
|---|---|
| **ID** | O29 |
| **Output** | Workflow Diagram |
| **Purpose** | Visual representation of the designed workflow showing tasks, agents, human gates, dependencies, data flows, and authority boundaries — making the workflow's structure visible and communicable to all stakeholders |
| **Quality Metrics** | Completeness: all tasks, agents, human gates, and dependencies shown; Clarity: a non-technical stakeholder can trace the flow; Accuracy: diagram matches the specification (no phantom tasks, no missing dependencies); Stakeholder-appropriateness: different views for different stakeholders |

### What the Diagram Contains

- **Tasks** (typed by cognitive operation: mechanical, analytical, generative, evaluative, intuitive)
- **Agents** (typed by class×orientation with colour coding)
- **Human gates** (decision points where the principal retains authority)
- **Dependencies** (task-to-task, agent-to-task, task-to-output)
- **Data flows** (what information passes between tasks and agents)
- **Authority boundaries** (what each agent CAN and CANNOT do — shown as boundary lines)
- **Progressive autonomy** (current level per agent, shown as stage indicator)

### Why It's a Separate Output, Not Just a View

It would be easy to say "the diagram is just a derived view of O24 (Coalition Specification)." But that undersells it. The diagram is:

1. **A design tool** — creating it reveals gaps in the specification that text doesn't surface (orphan tasks, circular dependencies, missing handoffs)
2. **A communication artefact** — the principal can review the diagram and spot problems in 30 seconds that would take 30 minutes to find in a document
3. **A stakeholder surface** — different stakeholders need different diagram views:
   - **Principal** sees tasks + human gates + authority boundaries (decision-focused)
   - **Implementer** sees agents + data flows + handoff protocols (build-focused)
   - **Assessor** sees quality metrics + health indicators + assessment points (monitoring-focused)

### Diagram Quality Criteria

The diagram must pass a simple test: **can someone who didn't design this workflow explain it back to you after looking at the diagram for 60 seconds?** If not, the diagram is too complex or poorly structured.

---

## Updated Output Count: 29

| ID | Output | Purpose |
|---|---|---|
| O24 | Coalition Specification | How agents work together (composition, handoff patterns) |
| O25 | Integration Contract | How the workflow integrates with external systems |
| O26 | Health Monitoring Dashboard | Real-time and periodic health metrics |
| **O29** | **Workflow Diagram** | **Visual representation of the workflow structure** |

O29 has dependencies on O5 (task contracts), O6 (authority guards), O24 (coalition spec), and O19 (handoff protocol) — it visualises what those outputs specify.

---

Updated memory with O29.The diagram also solves a practical problem: when you present a workflow specification to someone like Sarah Chen, she doesn't want to read 12 pages. She wants to *see* it. The diagram IS the boundary map for the workflow itself — showing where each agent's authority starts and stops, where human judgment is required, and how information flows.

Ready for Phase 2 (Backcasting) whenever you are.

### Prompt

phase 2

### Response

# Phase 2: Backcasting (TRACE)

*Working backward from the 29 outputs. What inputs does each output need? Where do they come from? Where are the gaps?*

---

## Method

For each output, I trace:
1. **Input Requirements** — what information is needed to produce this output
2. **Source** — where does each input come from (another output, external input, or CAWDP process)
3. **Criticality** — must-have (gates production) vs should-have (shapes quality) vs nice-to-have (refines over time)

Then I map the full dependency graph and identify the critical path.

---

## Input Requirements by Output Group

### Group 1: Identity (O1-O4) — Starting Points

These are the most upstream outputs. They depend primarily on external inputs.

| Output | Input Requirement | Source | Criticality |
|---|---|---|---|
| **O1** Workflow Identity Card | Principal's intent and purpose | External: human judgment | Must-have |
| | Answers to 4 identity questions | External: human judgment | Must-have |
| | Domain context | External: domain expertise | Must-have |
| **O2** Possibility Statement | Principal's vision for what's possible | External: human judgment | Must-have |
| | Domain outcome patterns | External: domain expertise | Should-have |
| | Pattern library (matching archetypes) | External: CAWDP pattern system | Should-have |
| **O3** Principal Declaration | Who the principal is and what authority they retain | External: human judgment | Must-have |
| | User context (9-category framework) | External: user context capture | Should-have |
| **O4** Scope Boundary Map | What's in scope and out of scope | External: human judgment | Must-have |
| | Domain workflow patterns | External: domain expertise | Should-have |
| | O2 (possibility statement) | O2 | Must-have |

**Key finding:** Group 1 outputs depend almost entirely on external human input. No internal output dependencies (they're the seeds). The principal's judgment is the root input for the entire dependency tree.

---

### Group 2: Contracts (O5-O8)

| Output | Input Requirement | Source | Criticality |
|---|---|---|---|
| **O5** Task Contract Schema | Task decomposition (what tasks exist) | CAWDP Phase 3 output | Must-have |
| | O1 (identity) | O1 | Must-have |
| | O4 (scope boundary map) | O4 | Must-have |
| | Agent class authority boundary patterns | External: CAWDP taxonomy | Should-have |
| **O6** Authority Guard Schema | O1 (identity — what each agent IS) | O1 | Must-have |
| | O3 (principal declaration — who has authority) | O3 | Must-have |
| | O5 (task contracts — what needs guarding) | O5 | Must-have |
| | Failure mode analysis per agent | CAWDP Phase 5 output | Must-have |
| | Agent class × orientation patterns | External: CAWDP taxonomy | Should-have |
| **O7** Cost Budget Framework | O5 (task contracts — what tasks cost) | O5 | Must-have |
| | O6 (authority guards — what enforcement costs) | O6 | Should-have |
| | Agent class cost benchmarks | External: per-class token estimates | Should-have |
| | LLM pricing data | External: current pricing | Nice-to-have |
| **O8** State Machine Contract | O5 (task contracts — what tasks exist per agent) | O5 | Must-have |
| | O6 (authority guards — what authority changes per level) | O6 | Must-have |
| | O7 (cost budget — what budgets change per level) | O7 | Should-have |

**Key finding:** O5 (Task Contract Schema) depends on Phase 3 (Task Decomposition) output. O6 (Authority Guards) depends on Phase 5 (Event Storming / Failure Mode Analysis). This means Groups 2 and 3 can't be fully produced until later CAWDP phases have run. **Contracts formalize what decomposition discovers and failure analysis reveals.**

---

### Group 3: Behaviour (O9-O12)

|---|---|---|---|

| | O8 (state machine — what states exist) | O8 | Must-have |
| | Trust requirements per agent | External: human judgment | Must-have |
| **O12** Specification Aging Schedule | O9 (directives — specifications to track) | O9 | Must-have |
| | O11 (progressive autonomy — review alignment) | O11 | Should-have |
| | Agent class aging benchmarks | External: CAWDP class benchmarks | Should-have |

**Key finding:** Behaviour outputs form a tight dependency chain: O1→O9→O12 and O1+O6→O10 and O1+O6+O8→O11. O10 requires failure modes from Phase 5. Behaviour is where identity becomes operational.

### Group 4: Assessment (O13-O15) — The Structural Innovation

| Output | Input Requirement | Source | Criticality |
|---|---|---|---|
| **O13** Outcome Metrics Framework | O2 (possibility statement — what outcomes are possible) | O2 | Must-have |

**Key finding:** Assessment outputs form their own chain: O13→O14→O15. O13 depends on O2 and O5 — it needs to know what outcomes are possible and what tasks produce them. This creates a dependency pattern where assessment is designed alongside the workflow, not bolted on after.

### Group 5: Implementation (O16-O19)

| Output | Input Requirement | Source | Criticality |
|---|---|---|---|

| | O5 (task contracts — what knowledge tasks require) | O5 | Must-have |
| | Domain knowledge sources | External: domain expertise | Must-have |
| | Epistemic metadata requirements | External: CC-3 specification | Should-have |

| | O6 (authority guards — what information can flow) | O6 | Should-have |

**Key finding:** O16 (Implementation Config) depends on O17 and O18, but O17 and O18 depend on O5 and O6. The implementation chain runs: O5+O6 → O17+O18 → O16. O19 depends on O24, creating a cross-group dependency.

---

### Group 6: Human Artefacts (O20-O23)

| Output | Input Requirement | Source | Criticality |
|---|---|---|---|
| **O20** Cognitive Load Budget | O3 (principal declaration — who the human is) | O3 | Must-have |
| | O5 (task contracts — what requires human attention) | O5 | Must-have |
| | User's cognitive capacity | External: user context | Should-have |
| **O21** Override Protocol | O6 (authority guards — where boundaries are) | O6 | Must-have |
| | O8 (state machine — what states agents can be in) | O8 | Must-have |
| | O3 (principal declaration — who has override authority) | O3 | Must-have |
| **O22** Interface Specification | O20 (cognitive load — how much attention available) | O20 | Must-have |
| | O21 (override protocol — when humans intervene) | O21 | Must-have |
| | O3 (principal declaration — who the human is) | O3 | Must-have |
| | User context (9-category framework) | External: user context | Should-have |
| **O23** Enrichment Assessment | O1-O4 (identity — what the workflow is for) | O1-O4 | Must-have |
| | O20-O22 (other human artefacts) | O20-O22 | Must-have |
| | O13 (outcome metrics — what outcomes to measure against) | O13 | Should-have |
| | Human's own assessment of enrichment | External: human judgment | Must-have |

**Key finding:** Human artefacts have a chain: O3+O5→O20, O3+O6+O8→O21, O20+O21+O3→O22, O1-O4+O20-O22+O13→O23. O23 (Enrichment Assessment) depends on the most inputs — it's the last human artefact produced.

---

## Three Key Findings from Backcasting

### Finding 1: The Assessment Spiral

O13 (Outcome Metrics) depends on O2 (Possibility Statement) and O5 (Task Contracts). But O5 depends on Phase 3 (Task Decomposition), which depends on knowing what outcomes to decompose toward. And knowing what outcomes matter comes from O13.

This is NOT a circular dependency. It's a **spiral**:

```
O2 (rough possibility)
  → O13 (rough outcome metrics — what "good" looks like at possibility level)
    → informs Phase 3 (decompose toward those outcomes)
      → O5 (task contracts based on decomposition)
        → O13 refined (precise outcome metrics tied to specific tasks)
```

The first pass through O13 is possibility-level (what outcomes COULD we measure?). The second pass is task-level (what outcomes WILL we measure for each task?). This is healthy — it means assessment gets more precise as the design progresses.

**Implication:** The meta-workflow should produce O13 in two passes — a rough version after O2 and a refined version after O5.

### Finding 2: Phase 3 and Phase 5 Are Gatekeeper Phases

Five outputs have hard dependencies on CAWDP process outputs:
- O5 depends on Phase 3 (Task Decomposition)
- O6 depends on Phase 5 (Event Storming / Failure Modes)
- O10 depends on Phase 5 (Failure Modes)

This means Phase 3 and Phase 5 are **gatekeepers** — without their outputs, the contract and behaviour groups can't be produced. The meta-workflow must ensure these phases produce their outputs before the workflow attempts to generate O5, O6, and O10.

**Implication:** The pipeline architecture (Phase 6) must enforce that Phase 3 and Phase 5 complete before O5/O6/O10 generation begins. These are structural dependencies, not ordering preferences.

### Finding 3: Two Dependency Tiers

The 29 outputs fall into two tiers based on dependency depth:

**Tier 1 — Direct from Identity (6 outputs):**
O1, O2, O3, O4, O7, O12
These can be produced from identity outputs + external inputs. They don't depend on task decomposition or failure analysis. They're the "early harvest" — valuable outputs that can be produced before the full design is complete.

**Tier 2 — Requires Process Outputs (23 outputs):**
O5, O6, O8-O11, O13-O29
These depend on Phase 3 or Phase 5 outputs. They require the full CAWDP process to have progressed through task decomposition and failure analysis before they can be produced.

**Implication:** The meta-workflow should produce Tier 1 outputs early as milestone artifacts (the "Boundary Map" equivalent), then produce Tier 2 outputs as the design progresses. This matches the consulting Boundary Journey structure — early value, then deepening.

---

## Critical Path

The longest dependency chain from external input to final output:

```
Human Judgment
  → O1 (Identity)
    → O5 (Task Contracts) [via Phase 3]
      → O6 (Authority Guards) [via Phase 5]
        → O8 (State Machine)
          → O11 (Progressive Autonomy)
            → O14 (Element Performance)
              → O15 (Refinement Protocol)
                → O26 (Health Monitoring)
                  → O28 (Operations Manual)
```

**10 steps from human judgment to Operations Manual.** This is the critical path. If any step is delayed, O28 is delayed. The critical path runs through:
1. Identity capture (O1)
2. Task decomposition (Phase 3 → O5)
3. Failure analysis (Phase 5 → O6)
4. State machine design (O8)
5. Progressive autonomy design (O11)
6. Assessment chain (O13 → O14 → O15)
7. Health monitoring (O26)
8. Operations manual (O28)

---

## Gap Analysis

### Inputs With No Source (Requires External Input)

| Input | Needed By | Source Type |
|---|---|---|
| Principal's intent and purpose | O1, O2, O3 | Human judgment — irreplaceable |
| Domain expertise | O1, O2, O4, O10, O18 | External — must be provided or discovered |
| Failure mode analysis | O6, O10 | CAWDP Phase 5 — process output |
| Task decomposition | O5 | CAWDP Phase 3 — process output |
| Platform capabilities | O16, O17 | External — framework research |
| Human's definition of "excellent" | O13 | Human judgment — irreplaceable |
| User context | O20, O22 | External — must be captured |
| Agent class patterns | O5, O6, O11, O12, O14 | CAWDP pattern library — can be pre-built |

### No Gaps in Internal Dependencies

Every internal dependency (output → output) has a source. The dependency graph is complete — no orphan outputs, no missing inputs. This validates the Phase 1 output specification: the 29 outputs form a closed system where every output's inputs are satisfied by other outputs or explicitly identified external inputs.

---

## Input Requirements as a Check on Phase 1

The backcasting revealed two adjustments to Phase 1:

**1. O13 (Outcome Metrics) needs two-pass production**

O13 depends on both O2 (early) and O5 (later). It should be produced in two passes:
- **O13a** (rough): after O2 — what outcomes COULD we measure? (possibility-level)
- **O13b** (refined): after O5 — what outcomes WILL we measure? (task-level)

This is the Assessment Spiral in action. O13a is the rough map; O13b is the detailed specification.

**2. O6 (Authority Guards) needs failure mode input**

O6 depends on failure mode analysis from Phase 5. But O6 is in Group 2 (Contracts), which is logically "early" in the output sequence. The resolution: O6 is produced in two stages:
- **O6a** (structural): after O5 — what authority boundaries exist based on identity and task structure
- **O6b** (failure-tied): after Phase 5 — what authority boundaries are tied to specific failure modes

O6a provides the structural framework; O6b ties each boundary to its failure mode reasoning. Both are needed, but O6a can be produced earlier.

---

## Summary

| Element | Finding |
|---|---|
| External inputs | 8 types — human judgment (irreplaceable), domain expertise, CAWDP knowledge, pattern library, platform capabilities, user context, pricing data, class benchmarks |
| Output dependencies | 29 outputs with 52 identified dependencies — no orphan outputs, no missing sources |
| Critical path | 10 steps: Human Judgment → O1 → Phase 3 → O5 → Phase 5 → O6 → O8 → O11 → O14 → O15 → O26 → O28 |
| Assessment spiral | O13 produced in two passes (rough after O2, refined after O5) |
| Gatekeeper phases | Phase 3 (task decomposition) and Phase 5 (failure analysis) block O5/O6/O10 |
| Two-pass outputs | O6 (structural then failure-tied) and O13 (rough then refined) |
| Tier 1 early harvest | O1, O2, O3, O4, O7, O12 — valuable before full design completes |
| Tier 2 deep design | 23 outputs requiring Phase 3/5 completion |

## Run 4 — Agentic Expert ✓ COMPLETED

*2026-05-19T08:19:01* · `glm-5.1:cloud` · `f0aa250e...`

### Prompt

proceed with phase 3

### Response

# Phase 3: Task Decomposition (DECOMPOSE)

*Decomposing toward known outputs. Every task traces to a specific output.*

---

## Decomposition Approach

Following CAWDP's revised order: I'm decomposing toward known destinations (the 29 outputs from Phase 1), along traced paths (the dependencies from Phase 2), not into a vacuum.

Each task gets:
- **ID**: T{group}.{sequence}
- **Name**: Plain English
- **Cognitive Type**: Mechanical / Analytical / Generative / Evaluative / Intuitive
- **Produces**: Which output(s) it contributes to
- **Requires**: Which inputs it needs
- **Failure Mode**: What "wrong" looks like for this task
- **Notes**: Anything that distinguishes this task's character

---

## Group 1: Identity Tasks

These produce the seeds — O1, O2, O3, O4. Almost entirely intuitive/human judgment with agent support (reflection, structuring, pattern matching).

| ID | Task Name | Cognitive Type | Produces | Requires | Failure Mode | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| T1.1 | Discover principal's purpose | Intuitive | O1, O2 | Human judgment | Purpose is vague or borrowed, not genuinely owned | Possibility-oriented. The agent mirrors, never decides. |
| T1.2 | Identify what this workflow IS | Intuitive | O1 | Human judgment, T1.1 | Identity answer describes behaviour, not essence | One of the 4 identity questions. Agent must not suggest — only reflect. |
| T1.3 | Determine possibility stance | Evaluative | O1 | Human judgment, T1.1, pattern library | Stance is assigned by agent, not discovered from human | Second identity question. Explore/Advise/Stay-in-Lane selection. |
| T1.4 | Discover what would violate identity | Intuitive | O1, O10 | Human judgment, T1.2 | Never-rules are generic ("be helpful") rather than identity-specific | Third identity question. Hardest boundary moments come from here. |
| T1.5 | Identify what would make it WRONG | Evaluative | O1, O10, O13 | Human judgment, T1.2, T1.4 | Failure description is too generic or describes total failure, not plausible wrong | Fourth identity question. "Worst failure that looks like success" — the meta-failure. |
| T1.6 | Articulate possibility statement | Generative | O2 | T1.1-T1.5 | Statement is constraint-oriented, not possibility-oriented | Agent drafts, human validates. Must stay expansive. |
| T1.7 | Declare principal authority | Evaluative | O3 | Human judgment | Authority is implicit, not explicit | Who retains decision authority? What's delegated? What's never delegated? |
| T1.8 | Draw scope boundaries | Analytical | O4 | T1.6, T1.7, domain expertise | Scope is too broad (everything) or too narrow (trivial) | Explicit in-scope AND out-of-scope items. At least 3 out-of-scope. |

**Group 1 notes:**
- T1.1-T1.5 are the 4 identity questions in operational form, with T1.1 as the gateway conversation.
- The possibility-oriented agent stance (CC-9) is critical here. These tasks operate in IMAGINE mode. Feasibility belongs in later phases.
- T1.4 and T1.5 produce identity-level constraints that feed directly into O10 (Behavioural Boundaries) and O13 (Outcome Metrics).
- T1.3 uses the 3-orientation selection (Possibility / Bridge / Constraint) as a design parameter, not a label.

---

## Group 2: Assessment Foundation Tasks

These produce O13a (rough Outcome Metrics) — the early pass that creates the assessment spiral. Produced after Group 1 but before task decomposition.

| ID | Task Name | Cognitive Type | Produces | Requires | Failure Mode | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| T2.1 | Define "excellent outcomes" | Intuitive | O13a | T1.1-T1.5, human judgment | Definition is vague ("good results") rather than specific and measurable | Human's genuine standard of excellence. Agent reflects, never defines. |
| T2.2 | Identify outcome dimensions | Analytical | O13a | T2.1, O2, pattern library | Dimensions are chosen from template, not discovered from domain | What aspects of the workflow's output constitute excellence? |
| T2.3 | Draft initial metrics | Generative | O13a | T2.1, T2.2 | Metrics are vanity metrics (activity, not outcome) rather than impact metrics | Agent drafts candidate metrics, human selects/refines. |
| T2.4 | Validate metric-measurability | Evaluative | O13a | T2.3 | Metrics can't actually be measured in practice | "Can we collect this data? Will it tell us what we need to know?" |

**Group 2 notes:**
- This is O13a — the rough pass. It's produced BEFORE task decomposition, because it tells us what outcomes to decompose TOWARD.
- T2.1 is where "measurably" and "ongoing refinement" become concrete. The human's definition of excellent outcomes is the North Star for everything that follows.
- This group IS the Assessment Spiral's first turn: possibility-level metrics that will be refined in O13b after O5.

---

## Group 3: Decomposition Tasks

These are CAWDP Phase 3's core tasks — decomposing the workflow into tasks, subtasks, and cognitive operations.

| ID | Task Name | Cognitive Type | Produces | Requires | Failure Mode | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---| 
| T3.1 | Decompose workflow into phases | Analytical | O5 (partial) | T1.8 (scope), T2.1-T2.4 (outcomes), CAWDP phase model | Phases are copied from CAWDP template rather than emerging from the workflow's identity | The workflow follows CAWDP's phase sequence but the phases are specific to THIS workflow, not generic. |
| T3.2 | Identify subtasks per phase | Analytical | O5 (partial) | T3.1, T2.1-T2.4 | Subtasks are too granular (micro-tasks) or too abstract (phases-as-tasks) | Each subtask should be assignable to a cognitive type. |
| T3.3 | Classify subtask cognitive types | Analytical | O5 (partial) | T3.2 | Classification is forced into one type when a subtask is genuinely multi-type | Mechanical, Analytical, Generative, Evaluative, Intuitive. Multi-type subtasks need splitting. |
| T3.4 | Map subtasks to outputs | Analytical | O5 (complete) | T3.2, T3.3, O1-O4 | A subtask doesn't trace to any output (orphan task) or an output has no producing subtask (orphan output) | Every subtask traces to a specific output. Every output has at least one producing subtask. This is the traceability check. |
| T3.5 | Identify subtask dependencies | Analytical | O5 (partial), O24 (partial) | T3.4 | Dependencies are assumed rather than traced from output requirements | Maps directly from Phase 2 dependency graph. |
| T3.6 | Identify data flows between subtasks | Analytical | O19 (partial) | T3.5 | Data flows are assumed to be prose descriptions rather than typed schemas | Every data flow should be typed (contract schema, not "a document"). |

**Group 3 notes:**
- T3.1-T3.4 are the core decomposition chain. T3.4 (traceability check) is the quality gate — no orphans in either direction.
- T3.3 requires multi-type subtasks to be split. "Review and decide" is two subtasks (evaluate then intuit), not one.
- T3.6 starts building O19 (Handoff Protocol) early — data flows are the beginning of handoff design.

---

## Group 4: Boundary and Guard Tasks

These produce O6a (structural authority boundaries), O10a (structural behavioural boundaries), and feed into Phase 5 for failure-tied versions.

| ID | Task Name | Cognitive Type | Produces | Requires | Failure Mode | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| T4.1 | Identify authority boundaries | Evaluative | O6a, O10a | T1.4, T1.5, T3.4 | Boundaries are generic ("be careful") rather than identity-specific | Derived from T1.4 (what violates identity) and T1.5 (what would make it wrong). |
| T4.2 | Map boundaries to failure modes | Analytical | O6a, O10a | T4.1, T1.5 | Boundary doesn't address a specific failure mode | Every boundary must address a specific failure. No decorative boundaries. |
| T4.3 | Classify enforcement regime | Evaluative | O6a | T4.2 | Everything is Declare (Regime 1) when some need Prevent (Regime 3) | Declare/Detect/Prevent classification for each boundary. |
| T4.4 | Define harm assessment | Evaluative | O6a, O10a | T4.1, T4.2 | Harm assessment is skipped for "low-risk" workflows | Every workflow gets a harm assessment. Indirect data harm is still harm. |
| T4.5 | Draft structural guard schemas | Generative | O6a, O10a | T4.1-T4.4 | Guards are prompt-level (Regime 1) when data-level (Regime 3) is needed | Agent drafts, human validates. |

**Group 4 notes:**
- T4.3 (enforcement regime classification) is where CC-4 Information Boundaries gets concrete. "Don't access other agents' data" can be Declare (prompt instruction), Detect (monitoring), or Prevent (database-level enforcement).
- T4.4 ensures harm assessment isn't skipped — even the Project Manager agent (low-risk Aggregator) had indirect operational harm through data integrity failures.
- O6a and O10a are structural (identity and scope-based). O6b and O10b (failure-tied) come after Phase 5.

---

## Group 5: Assessment Design Tasks (Refined Pass)

These produce O13b (refined Outcome Metrics), O14 (Element Performance Dashboard), and O15 (Refinement Protocol). This is the Assessment Spiral's second turn — now with task decomposition and boundaries available.

| ID | Task Name | Cognitive Type | Produces | Requires | Failure Mode | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| T5.1 | Refine metrics to specific tasks | Analytical | O13b | T2.1-T2.4 (O13a), T3.4 (task decomposition) | Metrics are too abstract to measure specific task outcomes | O13a gave us possibility-level metrics; O13b ties them to specific tasks. |
| T5.2 | Design element performance criteria | Generative | O14 | T5.1, T4.1 (boundaries), O5 | Criteria measure agent/phase performance, not just workflow output | Every agent and phase gets performance criteria. |
| T5.3 | Define refinement triggers and cadences | Analytical | O15 | T5.2, O12 (when available), O8 (when available) | Triggers are only calendar-based, not event-based | Both periodic review and event-driven triggers (drift, override spikes, cost overruns). |
| T5.4 | Design assessment feedback loop | Generative | O15 | T5.2, T5.3 | Assessment data doesn't connect to specific design decisions | The EVOLVE loop must be closed: assessment → specific design decision → redesign. |
| T5.5 | Validate closed-loop refinement | Evaluative | O15 | T5.4 | Can't trace from measurement → action → improvement | Can someone follow the loop from "metric dropped" to "specific design change" to "metric improved"? |

**Group 5 notes:**
- This is where "measurably" and "ongoing refinement" become structural. O13a was possibility-level; O13b is task-level.
- T5.4 is the most important task in this group — if assessment data doesn't connect to design decisions, the EVOLVE loop is broken.
- T5.5 validates the loop is actually closed. If you can't trace from metric → action → improvement, the refinement protocol is decorative, not functional.

---

## Group 6: Contract Formalisation Tasks

These produce O5 (complete Task Contracts), O6b (failure-tied Authority Guards), O7 (Cost Budget), O8 (State Machine).

| ID | Task Name | Cognitive Type | Produces | Requires | Failure Mode | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| T6.1 | Formalise task contracts | Generative | O5 | T3.4, T4.1, O6a | Contracts are prose descriptions, not typed schemas | 8 primitives per task: Contract, Principal, Schema, Guard, Budget, Invocation, Revert, Event. |
| T6.2 | Tie authority guards to failure modes | Analytical | O6b | T4.1-T4.5, Phase 5 (failure mode analysis) | Guard is stated without reference to a specific failure mode | This is O6b — the failure-tied pass. Every guard addresses a specific failure. |
| T6.3 | Estimate per-agent cost budgets | Analytical | O7 | T6.1, agent class benchmarks | Budget is a single number without halt conditions or variance range | Per-class benchmarks: Extractor 1K-5K, Measurer 1K-5K, Assessor 5K-30K, Generator 10K-50K+, Aggregator 2K-10K. |
| T6.4 | Design progressive autonomy states | Generative | O8 | T6.2, T6.3, trust requirements | States are defined without measurable promotion/demotion criteria | Shadow → Advisory → Supervised → Autonomous. Every transition has criteria. |

**Group 6 notes:**
- T6.1 depends on Phase 3 output (task decomposition) AND T4.1 (structural boundaries). It's the synthesis task — bringing decomposition and boundaries together into formal contracts.
- T6.2 is the second pass through O6 — now with failure modes from Phase 5.
- T6.3 uses per-class token benchmarks from the template refinements.
- T6.4 designs the progressive autonomy state machine per dimension (not per agent — per dimension).

---

## Group 7: Behaviour Specification Tasks

These produce O9 (Directives), O10b (failure-tied Behavioural Boundaries), O11 (Progressive Autonomy Path), O12 (Aging Schedule).

| ID | Task Name | Cognitive Type | Produces | Requires | Failure Mode | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| T7.1 | Draft agent directives | Generative | O9 | T1.2, T1.3, T6.1, T6.2 | Directive describes behaviour, not identity — "be helpful" not "you ARE a boundary mapper" | Identity-first: directives derive from what the agent IS, not what it does. |
| T7.2 | Specify behavioural boundaries | Generative | O10b | T6.2, Phase 5 output | Boundary is stated but not tied to a failure mode or enforcement regime | Every boundary: what it is, why it exists, what failure it prevents, how it's enforced. |
| T7.3 | Define progressive autonomy path | Generative | O11 | T6.4, T7.2 | Path is generic ("earn trust over time") rather than per-dimension with measurable criteria | Per-dimension: some dimensions never go autonomous (access control). |
| T7.4 | Set specification aging cadences | Analytical | O12 | T7.1, T7.3, agent class benchmarks | Cadences are uniform rather than agent-class-specific | Extractors: 90 days. Measurers: 60 days. Assessors: 30 days. Generators: 14 days. |

**Group 7 notes:**
- T7.1 is where identity-first design becomes operational. The directive starts from "What IS this agent?" not "What does this agent do?"
- T7.3 must be per-dimension. The template refinements established this as default, not special case.
- T7.4 varies by agent class — high-stakes agents (Generators, Assessors) need more frequent review than low-stakes (Extractors, Measurers).

---

## Group 8: Implementation Specification Tasks

These produce O16 (Implementation Config), O17 (Tool Spec), O18 (Knowledge Config), O19 (Handoff Protocol).

| ID | Task Name | Cognitive Type | Produces | Requires | Failure Mode | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| T8.1 | Specify tool requirements | Analytical | O17 | T6.1, T6.2, platform capabilities | Tool is specified without authority boundary (what the tool CAN and CANNOT do) | Every tool has declared authority. |
| T8.2 | Specify knowledge requirements | Analytical | O18 | T1.2, T6.1, domain expertise | Knowledge is specified without epistemic metadata requirements | Every knowledge item has confidence, provenance, assumptions, limitations, recency, uncertainty (CC-3). |
| T8.3 | Design handoff protocols | Generative | O19 | T6.1, T6.2, O24 (coalition spec) | Handoff is prose description, not typed contract | Every handoff has a contract: what passes, in what format, with what quality, to whom. |
| T8.4 | Produce implementation configuration | Generative | O16 | T8.1, T8.2, T7.1, platform capabilities | Config is framework-specific when it should be platform-agnostic | What and why, not which tool. Litmus: could two teams on different frameworks produce equivalent agents from this spec? |

**Group 8 notes:**
- T8.4 must pass the platform-agnostic test: "Could two teams on different frameworks produce functionally equivalent agents from this specification?"
- T8.3 depends on O24 (Coalition Specification), which comes from Group 9. This is a cross-group dependency.
- T8.2 must include epistemic metadata requirements — every knowledge item specifies its quality requirements (CC-3).

## Group 9: Ecosystem Specification Tasks

These produce O24 (Coalition Specification), O25 (Integration Contract), O26 (Health Monitoring Dashboard), O29 (Workflow Diagram).

| ID | Task Name | Cognitive Type | Produces | Requires | Failure Mode | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| T9.1 | Determine composition pattern | Evaluative | O24 | T6.1, T6.2, T7.1, T7.2 | Composition is assumed (single agent) when it should be analyzed (workflow vs team) | Workflow vs Team vs Agent composition decision, justified not assumed. |
| T9.2 | Specify agent roles and interactions | Generative | O24 | T9.1, T7.1, T7.2 | Roles overlap or leave gaps (same task covered by two agents, or uncovered) | Every task traces to exactly one role. No overlaps, no gaps. |

**Group 9 notes:**
- T9.1 is the composition decision — a genuinely evaluative task. It must be justified, not assumed.
- T9.5 produces O29 (Workflow Diagram) — the visual artefact requested. It must show authority boundaries, not just task flow.
- T9.6 is the quality test for O29 — the 60-second explanation test.

Measurable: "can the human do X they couldn't before? Can they do Y faster? Can they see Z they couldn't see?" |

**Group 10 notes:**
- T10.1 must account for real cognitive constraints — interruption recovery (23 min), decision fatigue, context switching cost. Not ideal conditions.

---

## Three Key Findings from Task Decomposition

### Finding 1: The Assessment Spiral Requires Two Passes

O13 (Outcome Metrics) cannot be produced in a single pass. It requires:
- **Pass 1 (O13a)**: After Group 1 — possibility-level metrics based on identity and scope
- **Pass 2 (O13b)**: After Groups 3-4 — task-level metrics tied to specific agents and phases

This means the meta-workflow must revisit assessment after decomposition, not treat it as a one-time output. This is structurally healthy — it means assessment gets more precise as the design progresses.

### Finding 2: Boundaries Are Produced in Two Passes Too

O6 (Authority Guards) and O10 (Behavioural Boundaries) each require:
- **Structural pass (O6a, O10a)**: After Group 1 — identity-based boundaries from what the workflow IS and what would violate it
- **Failure-tied pass (O6b, O10b)**: After Phase 5 — boundaries tied to specific failure modes

This parallels the Assessment Spiral — boundaries start from identity and deepen through failure analysis.

### Finding 3: Group 1 and Group 2 Are the "Boundary Map" Equivalent

Groups 1-2 (12 tasks producing O1-O4 and O13a) are the meta-workflow's equivalent of the consulting Boundary Map — the early-harvest artifact that provides value before the full design is complete. If someone only completes Groups 1-2, they still have:
- What the workflow IS (O1)
- What outcomes it could achieve (O2)
- Who has authority (O3)
- What's in and out of scope (O4)
- What "excellent" looks like (O13a)

This is the 30-minute Boundary Map equivalent — identity, possibility, authority, scope, and excellence criteria.

Ready for Phase 4 (Capability Allocation) when you are — assigning each task to Human, Agent, or System, with complementarity gap analysis.

### Response

# Phase 4: Capability Allocation (ALLOCATE)

*Who does what? Ternary H/A/S analysis with complementarity gaps.*

---

## Method

For each task:
1. **Score** each actor (H/A/S) on 1-10 for their capability to perform the task
2. **Calculate gap** between best actor and next best
3. **Allocate** based on gap: ≥6 = human-only, 3-5 = collaborative (A prepares, H decides), ≤2 = agent-primary
4. **Assign decision authority** — who has final say
5. **Note failure mode** — what happens if the wrong actor is primary

---

## Full Allocation Table

### Group 1: Identity Tasks

| ID | Task | Type | H | A | S | Gap | Allocation | Authority | If Wrong Actor |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| T1.1 | Discover principal's purpose | Intuitive | 10 | 2 | 0 | 8 | **Human-only** | Human | Agent suggests purpose → specification capture |
| T1.2 | Identify what this workflow IS | Intuitive | 10 | 2 | 0 | 8 | **Human-only** | Human | Agent defines identity → template-filling, not genuine |
| T1.3 | Determine possibility stance | Evaluative | 8 | 4 | 1 | 4 | **Collaborative** | Human | Agent assigns stance → wrong orientation for the workflow |
| T1.4 | Discover what would violate identity | Intuitive | 10 | 3 | 0 | 7 | **Human-only** | Human | Agent generates never-rules → generic boundaries, not identity-specific |
| T1.5 | Identify what would make it WRONG | Evaluative | 9 | 4 | 0 | 5 | **Collaborative** | Human | Agent defines failure → describes total failure, not plausible wrong |
| T1.6 | Articulate possibility statement | Generative | 7 | 6 | 1 | 1 | **Collaborative** | Human | Agent drafts alone → constraint-oriented, not possibility-oriented |
| T1.7 | Declare principal authority | Evaluative | 10 | 3 | 0 | 7 | **Human-only** | Human | Agent declares authority → authority misaligned with principal |
| T1.8 | Draw scope boundaries | Analytical | 7 | 5 | 2 | 2 | **Collaborative** | Human | Agent draws scope alone → scope misaligned with domain reality |

**Group 1 pattern:** Identity tasks are heavily human-primary. The agent mirrors, reflects, and structures — but the human's judgment is irreplaceable for purpose, identity, never-rules, and authority declaration. T1.6 is the notable exception where agent drafts and human validates, because the human's intuition needs structuring into a statement.

| **Human-only** (gap ≥ 6) | **10** | T1.1, T1.2, T1.4, T1.7, T2.1, T4.1, T4.4, T5.5, T9.6, T10.4 |
| **Collaborative** (A prepares, H decides) | **31** | T1.3, T1.5, T1.6, T1.8, T2.2-T2.4, T3.1-T3.2, T4.2-T4.3, T4.5, T5.1-T5.4, T6.2, T6.4, T7.1-T7.3, T8.1-T8.3, T9.1-T9.4, T10.1-T10.3, T11.1 |
| **Agent-primary** (A produces, H spot-checks) | **11** | T3.3-T3.6, T6.1, T6.3, T7.4, T8.4, T9.5, T11.2 |
| **System** | **0** | (System handles infrastructure: enforcement, persistence, automation — not design tasks) |

---

## Pattern Analysis

### Pattern 1: Intuitive Tasks Are Human-Only

Every task typed as **Intuitive** (T1.1, T1.2, T1.4, T2.1) is human-only. This validates CAWDP's cognitive operation classification — intuitive tasks require genuine human judgment that cannot be delegated.

### Pattern 2: Evaluative Tasks Split Three Ways

Evaluative tasks fall into three categories:
- **Human-only** (gap ≥ 6): T1.7, T4.1, T4.4, T9.6, T10.4 — domain-specific evaluation only the principal can make
- **Collaborative** (gap 3-5): T1.3, T1.5, T4.3, T5.5 — agent prepares options/scenarios, human decides
- **Collaborative** (gap 1-2): T9.1 — agent analyses, human decides on composition pattern

The split depends on **domain specificity**: evaluative tasks that require domain expertise (harm assessment, authority boundaries, enrichment assessment) are human-only. Evaluative tasks where the agent can prepare options (possibility stance, harm scenarios, clarity validation) are collaborative.

### Pattern 3: Analytical Tasks Are Agent-Primary

Every task typed as **Analytical** with gap ≤ 2 (T3.3-T3.6, T7.4) is agent-primary. The agent is faster and more consistent at classification, mapping, tracing, and benchmarking. The human spot-checks for domain relevance.

### Pattern 4: Generative Tasks Are Collaborative

Every task typed as **Generative** is collaborative. The agent drafts; the human validates. This is the core of "agent prepares judgment, human makes judgment" applied to the design process itself.

### Pattern 5: The Agent's Role Shifts Across Phases

| Phase Group | Agent Role | Human Role |
|---|---|---|
| Groups 1-2 (Identity + Assessment Foundation) | **Mirror** — reflects, structures, never decides | **Decider** — provides genuine judgment |
| Groups 3-4 (Decomposition + Boundaries) | **Analyser** — traces, maps, classifies | **Validator** — spot-checks domain relevance |
| Groups 5-7 (Assessment + Contracts + Behaviour) | **Drafter** — drafts contracts, metrics, directives | **Approver** — approves or revises |
| Groups 8-11 (Implementation + Ecosystem + Human Experience + Deployment) | **Assembler** — produces configs, diagrams, manuals | **Reviewer** — Sarah Chen test, platform-agnostic test |

This shift mirrors the Possibility Orientation Principle: the agent starts as a possibility-oriented mirror and progressively becomes a constraint-oriented assembler.

---

## Human Gates

The 10 human-only tasks create **natural human gates** in the pipeline — points where the workflow must pause for human judgment before proceeding:

| Gate | After Tasks | Blocks | Decision Required |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Gate 1** | T1.1-T1.2 | Groups 2-11 | Is the identity genuine? (No template-filling) |
| **Gate 2** | T1.4, T1.7 | Groups 4-11 | Are the boundaries and authority genuinely the principal's? |
| **Gate 3** | T2.1 | Groups 3-5 | Is "excellent" genuinely defined? (Not generic) |
| **Gate 4** | T4.1, T4.4 | Groups 6-7 | Are the boundaries and harm assessment domain-specific? |
| **Gate 5** | T5.5 | Groups 6-7 | Does the assessment loop actually close? |
| **Gate 6** | T9.6 | Group 11 | Can someone explain the workflow in 60 seconds? |
| **Gate 7** | T10.4 | Group 11 | Is human creative freedom genuinely expanded? |

These gates are not optional checkpoints — they are structural requirements. The workflow cannot proceed past a gate until the human has made the required judgment.

---

## System Role

System tasks (enforcement, persistence, automation) are not design tasks — they are infrastructure:
- **Enforcement**: Regime 3 (Prevent) enforcement of authority boundaries (CC-4)
- **Persistence**: Storing all 29 outputs in the artefact graph (PRISM)
- **Automation**: Quality gate checks, dependency tracing, schema validation
- **Compilation**: Aggregating outputs into derived views (Operations Manual, Boundary Map, etc.)

System tasks are continuous, not sequential. They run alongside the human and agent tasks.

---

## Complementarity Insight

The allocation reveals that the meta-workflow IS the "agent prepares judgment, human makes judgment" principle applied to itself:

- **31 of 52 tasks are collaborative** (A prepares, H decides) — the majority of the workflow
- **10 tasks are human-only** — genuine judgment gates that cannot be delegated
- **11 tasks are agent-primary** — structured analytical/generative work the agent does faster and more consistently
- **0 tasks are system-primary** — design judgment is never automated

The workflow that designs workflows practises what it produces. The agent mirrors in identity, analyses in decomposition, drafts in specification, and assembles in implementation. The human decides on identity, validates on decomposition, approves on specification, and reviews on implementation.

---

Ready for Phase 5 (Event Storming) when you are — identifying what events could break this workflow and how to recover.

## Run 6 — Agentic Expert ✓ COMPLETED

*2026-05-19T12:18:32* · `glm-5.1:cloud` · `3d264b69...`

### Prompt

I need to push back on a lot of these. Especially T1.1, T1.2, T1.4, T2.1, T4.1. Humans have a cold start problem where we don't always know how to explain what we want so it is great to get help from the agent in defining that. We also don't always think about how things can go wrong if we haven't seen it go wrong. This doesn't align with the "agent prepares judgment, human makes judgment" principle. These should be collaborative rather than human-only. 

Please stop saying what things are 'not'. Just say what things are. I don't need the emphasis or embellishments. eg "These gates are not optional checkpoints" or "System tasks are continuous, not sequential." It just adds extra noise I don't need.

### Response

Good pushback. You're right — the cold start problem means the agent's preparation role is essential for those tasks, not optional. The agent helps you discover what you want, suggests patterns, offers scenarios you haven't thought of, reflects back what you say. That IS "agent prepares judgment, human makes judgment." Making those human-only contradicts the core principle.

The cold start correction shifts 10 tasks from human-only to collaborative. Every design task benefits from agent preparation — the agent asks the right questions, suggests patterns, offers scenarios, reflects back what it hears. The human makes the final judgment in every case.

---

## Human Gates

Collaborative tasks where the human's decision is the gate — the workflow pauses until the human makes the call.

| Gate | After Tasks | Blocks | Decision Required |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Gate 1** | T1.1-T1.2 | Groups 2-11 | Is the identity genuine? |
| **Gate 2** | T1.4, T1.7 | Groups 4-11 | Are the boundaries and authority correct? |
| **Gate 3** | T2.1 | Groups 3-5 | Is "excellent" defined specifically enough? |
| **Gate 4** | T4.1, T4.4 | Groups 6-7 | Are boundaries and harm assessment domain-specific? |
| **Gate 5** | T5.5 | Groups 6-7 | Does the assessment loop close? |
| **Gate 6** | T9.6 | Group 11 | Does the diagram pass the 60-second test? |
| **Gate 7** | T10.4 | Group 11 | Is human enrichment genuine? |

---

## Agent's Preparation Role by Task Type

| Task Type | Agent Preparation | Human Decision |
|---|---|---|
| Intuitive | Guided questions, pattern examples, reflection, scenarios | Final identity/purpose call |
| Evaluative | Option generation, scenario drafting, trade-off framing | Final judgment call |
| Analytical | Classification, mapping, tracing, benchmarking | Domain relevance spot-check |
| Generative | Drafting from captured inputs, structuring, compiling | Approval or revision |
| Aggregative | Compilation from all outputs, derived view generation | Validation (Sarah Chen test) |

### Response

# Phase 5: Event Storming (STRESS-TEST)

*What events could break this workflow? How do we recover?*

---

## Method

For each workflow stage, I identify:
1. **Domain events** — expected events as the workflow progresses
2. **Failure events** — what could go wrong
3. **Recovery path** — what to do when it goes wrong
4. **System trigger** — what the system detects and responds to automatically

---

## Discovery Phase (Groups 1-2: Identity + Assessment Foundation)

### Domain Events

| Event | Trigger | Produces |
|---|---|---|
| E1.1 Identity capture started | Human begins conversation with agent | Session initialised |
| E1.2 Pattern match found | Agent analyses initial input | Pattern suggestion cards |
| E1.3 Identity question answered | Human responds to identity question | Identity fragment captured |
| E1.4 Never-rule surfaced | Human identifies a boundary violation | Boundary fragment captured |
| E1.5 Possibility statement drafted | Agent synthesises identity fragments | O2 draft |
| E1.6 Authority declared | Human specifies who retains decision rights | O3 fragment |
| E1.7 Scope boundaries drawn | Human and agent agree in-scope/out-of-scope | O4 draft |
| E1.8 Excellence defined | Human articulates "what excellent looks like" | O13a draft |

### Failure Events

| Failure | Detection | Recovery | System Trigger |
|---|---|---|---|
| F1.1 Human gives vague identity answer | Agent cannot extract a concrete identity element after 2 attempts | Offer 3 archetypal examples from pattern library. Ask "which of these is closest?" | Pattern library query on vague input |
| F1.2 Human describes behaviour, asks for identity | Identity answer is "it does X" (behavioural) instead of "it IS X" (essence) | Reflect back: "You've described what it does. What IS it at its core?" Offer reframe. | Behaviour-language detection in identity answers |
| F1.3 Pattern match confidence below 40% | No pattern matches ≥ 40% confidence threshold | Switch to exploratory mode. Start with identity questions instead of pattern pre-fill. | Confidence score < 40% on pattern match |
| F1.4 Human cannot articulate "excellent" | O13a remains undefined after 3 prompts | Offer domain-specific outcome examples. Ask "which of these feels closest to your standard?" | 3 consecutive vague responses on T2.1 |
| F1.5 Scope is too broad | Scope boundary map includes > 10 in-scope items with no out-of-scope items | Present the scope boundary. Ask "if you had to remove 3 items, which would go?" | In-scope > 10 AND out-of-scope < 3 |
| F1.6 Scope is too narrow | Scope boundary map has < 3 in-scope items | Offer adjacent scope items from pattern library. Ask "would any of these add value?" | In-scope < 3 |

---

## Structure Phase (Groups 3-4: Decomposition + Boundaries)

### Domain Events

| Event | Trigger | Produces |
|---|---|---|
| E2.1 Decomposition started | Identity and scope approved (after Gate 1-3) | Task decomposition begins |
| E2.2 Phase identified | Agent proposes workflow phases | Phase list for review |
| E2.3 Subtask identified | Agent decomposes phase into subtasks | Subtask list with cognitive types |
| E2.4 Traceability gap found | Subtask has no output mapping | Gap flag raised |
| E2.5 Boundary identified | Human and agent agree on authority boundary | O6a fragment |
| E2.6 Harm category assigned | Agent and human classify harm type | Harm assessment fragment |

### Failure Events

| Failure | Detection | Recovery | System Trigger |
|---|---|---|---|
| F2.1 Subtask has no output mapping | Traceability check finds orphan subtask | Agent proposes output mapping. Human confirms or revises. | Orphan subtask detected (subtask with no output) |
| F2.2 Output has no producing subtask | Traceability check finds orphan output | Agent proposes subtask to produce it. Human confirms or revises. | Orphan output detected (output with no producing subtask) |
| F2.3 Subtask is genuinely multi-type | Classification produces "evaluate-and-decide" as single type | Split into two subtasks (evaluate + decide). Flag for human review. | Multi-cognitive-type subtask detected |
| F2.4 Boundary is generic ("be careful") | Authority boundary has no specific failure mode reference | Offer domain-specific boundary examples. Ask "what specific problem does this boundary prevent?" | Boundary without failure mode reference |
| F2.5 Harm assessment skipped | Human marks workflow as "low risk" with no harm categories | Present the indirect harm categories (data integrity, operational cascading, reputational). Every workflow gets at least one harm category. | Harm assessment = empty |
| F2.6 Enforcement regime defaults to Declare | All boundaries classified as Declare (Regime 1) | Present the 3-regime spectrum. Ask "which boundaries would benefit from structural enforcement?" | 100% boundaries at Declare regime |

---

## Realisation Phase (Groups 5-8: Assessment + Contracts + Behaviour + Implementation)

### Domain Events

| Event | Trigger | Produces |
|---|---|---|
| E3.1 Metrics refined to tasks | O13a metrics mapped to specific tasks | O13b draft |
| E3.2 Task contract formalised | 8 primitives defined for a task | O5 fragment |
| E3.3 Guard tied to failure mode | Authority boundary connected to specific failure | O6b fragment |
| E3.4 Cost budget estimated | Per-agent token range calculated | O7 fragment |
| E3.5 Progressive autonomy states designed | Per-dimension state machine defined | O8 fragment |
| E3.6 Directives drafted | Identity-first instructions written | O9 draft |
| E3.7 Behavioural boundaries specified | Boundaries with failure mode + enforcement regime | O10b draft |
| E3.8 Tool requirements specified | Tools with authority boundaries | O17 fragment |
| E3.9 Knowledge requirements specified | Knowledge with epistemic metadata requirements | O18 fragment |
| E3.10 Handoff protocol designed | Typed schemas for inter-task data | O19 fragment |

### Failure Events

| Failure | Detection | Recovery | System Trigger |
|---|---|---|---|
| F3.1 Metric is vanity (activity, not outcome) | Metric measures volume (count, frequency) without measuring impact | Ask "if this number doubled, would the workflow be better? How would you know?" | Activity metric without impact metric |
| F3.2 Contract is prose, typed schema | Task contract describes intent without typed input/output schema | Offer schema template. Draft schema from prose description. | Contract schema fields = empty |
| F3.3 Guard has no enforcement mechanism | Authority boundary is stated but enforcement regime is Declare with no justification | Ask "what happens if this boundary is crossed? Who detects it? How?" Present Detect/Prevent options. | Declare regime with no justification |
| F3.4 Progressive autonomy lacks measurable criteria | State transition says "when trust is earned" without specific metric | Offer per-dimension criteria templates (override rate < X%, verification pass > Y%, cost variance < Z%). | Autonomy transition criteria = qualitative only |
| F3.5 Directive describes behaviour, skips identity | Directive starts with "You do X" instead of "You ARE X" | Reflect back: "This describes what the agent does. What IS the agent? Rewrite from identity." | Behaviour-first language in directives |
| F3.6 Knowledge has no epistemic metadata | Knowledge item has no confidence, provenance, or recency | Offer CC-3 template. Auto-populate where possible (provenance from source, recency from timestamp). | Epistemic metadata fields = empty |
| F3.7 Handoff is prose, typed contract | Handoff describes "passes a document" without typed schema | Draft typed schema from handoff description. Ask human to validate. | Handoff schema = empty |
| F3.8 Cost budget has no halt condition | Budget is a single number without threshold or halt mechanism | Add halt-on-budget-exhaustion. Add variance range. | Budget without halt condition |

---

## Validation Phase (Groups 9-11: Ecosystem + Human Experience + Deployment)

### Domain Events

| Event | Trigger | Produces |
|---|---|---|
| E4.1 Composition pattern decided | Workflow vs Team vs Agent decision made | O24 fragment |
| E4.2 Roles specified | Every task traces to exactly one role | O24 draft |
| E4.3 Health metrics designed | IDS, UPR, override rate, cost variance per agent | O26 draft |
| E4.4 Workflow diagram produced | Visual artefact showing tasks, agents, gates, data flows | O29 draft |
| E4.5 Diagram passes 60-second test | Human unfamiliar with workflow can explain it back | O29 approved |
| E4.6 Cognitive load estimated | Per-phase load accounting for interruptions | O20 draft |
| E4.7 Override mechanisms designed | Specific points, mechanisms, and post-override actions | O21 draft |
| E4.8 Enrichment assessed | Specific freedoms expanded, measurable | O23 draft |
| E4.9 Operations manual compiled | All outputs aggregated into operational reference | O28 draft |
| E4.10 Deployment plan designed | Progressive autonomy with measurable criteria | O27 draft |

### Failure Events

| Failure | Detection | Recovery | System Trigger |
|---|---|---|---|
| F4.1 Composition pattern assumed | Composition decision has no justification | Ask "why is this the right composition for this workflow's complexity?" | Composition decision without justification |
| F4.2 Roles overlap | Two agents assigned same task | Highlight overlap. Ask "who owns this task?" | Task assigned to > 1 role |
| F4.3 Roles have gaps | Task has no assigned role | Highlight gap. Ask "who handles this?" | Task with no assigned role |
| F4.4 Diagram fails 60-second test | Human cannot explain workflow back after viewing | Simplify. Remove detail until the structure is explainable. Add back progressively. | 60-second test failure |
| F4.5 Health metrics are activity counts | Metrics measure volume without measuring health | Offer IDS, UPR, override rate templates. Ask "which of these tells you whether the agent is healthy?" | Activity-count-only health metrics |
| F4.6 Cognitive load assumes ideal conditions | Load estimate has no interruption or fatigue allowance | Add 23-min interruption recovery cost. Add decision fatigue scaling. | Load estimate without interruption allowance |
| F4.7 Override mechanism is vague | Override says "human can intervene" without specific trigger or mechanism | Ask "at what specific point? Through what mechanism? What happens after?" | Override without specific trigger or mechanism |
| F4.8 Enrichment is aspirational | "Expands creative freedom" with no specific freedom identified | Ask "what specific thing can the human do now that they couldn't before? What can they see that they couldn't see?" | Aspirational enrichment without specific freedom |
| F4.9 Operations manual is a data dump | Manual is > 20 pages of undifferentiated content | Apply stakeholder surface. Produce principal view (3-5 pages), implementer view (detailed), assessor view (metrics). | Manual > 20 pages with no stakeholder surfaces |
| F4.10 Deployment jumps to autonomous | Deployment plan starts at Supervised or Autonomous | Add Shadow phase with measurable criteria for each transition. | Deployment without Shadow phase |

---

## Cross-Phase Failure Events

These failures can happen at any phase.

| Failure | Detection | Recovery | System Trigger |
|---|---|---|---|
| F5.1 Session interrupted | User closes browser or goes idle > 30 min | Session state persisted. "Welcome back" with attention map and "Continue" as primary action. | Idle > 30 min |
| F5.2 Agent and human disagree | Human rejects agent's prepared option | Capture rejection reasoning. Present alternative options. Never argue. | Human rejection of agent output |
| F5.3 Agent cannot process input | Input falls outside agent's schema or capability | Offer structured alternatives. Capture raw input for human review. | Input validation failure |
| F5.4 Contradiction between earlier and later decisions | Earlier fragment conflicts with later fragment | Surface contradiction. Present both fragments. Ask human to resolve. | Contradiction detected in artefact graph |
| F5.5 Workflow stalled at gate | Gate decision pending > 24 hours | Gentle nudge with context: "You left off at [gate]. Here's what's blocking." | Gate pending > 24 hours |
| F5.6 Cost budget exceeded mid-workflow | Token spend exceeds 50% of budget before 50% of outputs complete | Alert human. Offer options: continue with awareness, simplify remaining outputs, pause. | Budget > 50% consumed at < 50% completion |

---

## Recovery Path Pattern

Every recovery follows the same structure:

1. **Detect** — the system identifies the failure event
2. **Surface** — present the issue to human (with context, without blame)
3. **Offer** — present 2-3 structured options for resolution
4. **Capture** — record the failure event, the human's resolution, and the reasoning
5. **Resume** — continue from the recovery point with updated state

This is the Decision Presentation Pattern applied to failure recovery: the agent prepares resolution options, the human decides.

---

## Event Summary

| Category | Count |
|---|---|
| Domain events | 26 |
| Phase-specific failures | 22 |
| Cross-phase failures | 6 |
| Total failure events | 28 |
| System-enforced triggers | 28 (one per failure event) |

---

## Three Key Findings

### Finding 1: Every Failure Recovery Is a Decision

All 28 failure events follow the same pattern: agent prepares resolution options, human decides. This is CAWDP's core principle applied to failure recovery. The system detects and surfaces; the agent offers options; the human decides.

### Finding 2: Most Failures Are Cold Start Variants

F1.1 (vague identity), F1.4 (can't articulate excellent), F2.4 (generic boundary), F3.5 (behaviour-first directives), F4.7 (vague override), F4.8 (aspirational enrichment) are all the same pattern: the human needs agent preparation to overcome a cold start. The agent offers examples, archetypes, and structured options. The human's genuine judgment emerges through the interaction.

### Finding 3: System Triggers Enable Regime 2 Enforcement

The 28 system triggers move boundary enforcement from Regime 1 (Declare — tell the agent the rules) to Regime 2 (Detect — flag violations). F3.3 (Declare regime without justification), F3.4 (qualitative autonomy criteria), F3.8 (budget without halt) are specific cases where the system enforces that boundaries are specific and measurable, not decorative.
