## Specification Aging Schedule

### Review Cadences by Agent Class

| Agent | Review Cadence | Trigger-Based Review | Aging Signal |
|---|---|---|---|
| Mirror | Every 10 specifications | Pattern library update | Pattern match confidence declining |
| Decomposer | Every 5 specifications | New domain type encountered | Orphan count increasing |
| Boundary Analyst | Every 5 specifications | Regulatory change in client domain | Boundary specificity declining |
| Assessment Architect | Every 5 specifications | Metric collection failure | Loop validation failing |
| Contract Drafter | Every 3 specifications | Framework update (Agno, LLM change) | Schema validation failures |
| Assembler | Every 5 specifications | Stakeholder surface format change | 60-second test failures |

### Trigger-Based Review

| Trigger | What to Review | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Pattern match confidence declining | Mirror identity questions, pattern library | Reassess pattern templates. Update pattern weights. |
| Orphan count increasing | Decomposer task-output mappings | Reassess decomposition templates. Update mapping rules. |
| Boundary specificity declining | Boundary Analyst failure mode library | Reassess failure mode taxonomy. Add domain-specific examples. |
| Metric collection failure | Assessment Architect feedback loop | Reassess metric feasibility. Update collection mechanisms. |
| Schema validation failures | Contract Drafter templates | Update schemas to match current framework capabilities. |
| 60-second test failures | Assembler diagram templates | Simplify diagram format. Update stakeholder surfaces. |
| Override rate > 30% | All agents | Demote one autonomy level. Investigate override clustering. |
| Cost budget exceeded > 50% | Token usage patterns | Reassess token budgets. Identify expensive agents. |
| Human override clustering on specific agent | Specific agent specification | Review agent identity questions and authority boundaries. |

---

## EVOLVE Cycle

### From Design to Evolution

Phase 9 does not end. The EVOLVE cycle connects back to Phase 0:

```
P0 (Purpose & Vision)
  ↓
P1-P9 (Design)
  ↓
EVALUATE (Phase 9 testing)
  ↓
EVOLVE (Back to P0)
```

### When to EVOLVE

| Signal | What It Means | Action |
|---|---|---|
| Override rate consistently > 30% | Human is correcting the workflow frequently | Revisit P0 (purpose) — is the workflow solving the right problem? |
| Identity capture quality consistently < 80% | The pattern system isn't capturing genuine identity | Revisit P1 (output specification) — are identity questions the right outputs? |
| Boundary specificity consistently < 100% | Boundaries aren't referencing failure modes | Revisit P4 (capability allocation) — are failure modes mapped correctly? |
| New agent class needed | The 5-class taxonomy doesn't cover a real use case | Revisit P3 (task decomposition) — is the decomposition complete? |
| Framework update (Agno, LLM change) | Implementation environment changed | Revisit P6 (system architecture) — update implementation config |
| Stakeholder surface format change | Users need different views | Revisit P8 (human experience) — update surface designs |
| Cost budget consistently exceeded | The workflow costs more than expected | Revisit P4 (capability allocation) — are agent assignments optimal? |
| New domain type encountered | Pattern library doesn't cover a real use case | Add new pattern. Revisit P0 (purpose) — does the new domain affect scope? |

### EVOLVE Protocol

1. **Detect** — health monitoring identifies a signal
2. **Diagnose** — determine which phase(s) the signal affects
3. **Revisit** — return to the affected phase(s) with new information
4. **Propagate** — update all downstream phases affected by the change
5. **Re-validate** — run the affected hypotheses again
6. **Re-deploy** — update the specification with the evolved design

This is the spiral — Phase 9 cycles back to Phase 0 via EVOLVE. The workflow improves with every specification it produces, feeding health data back into design decisions.

---

## Parallel Test Track

Every design decision generates a test. Tests run in parallel with the workflow, not after it.

### Test Specification Template

| Field | Content |
|---|---|
| Test ID | T-[phase]-[number] |
| Phase Origin | Which CAWDP phase generated this test |
| Hypothesis | Which of H1-H7 this test validates |
| Test Target | What is being tested |
| Test Method | How the test is conducted |
| Pass Criteria | What constitutes a pass |
| Fail Criteria | What constitutes a fail |
| Priority | Critical / High / Medium / Low |

### Test Matrix

| Test ID | Phase Origin | Hypothesis | Test Target | Method | Pass | Fail | Priority |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| T-0-1 | P0 | H1 | Identity questions produce genuine answers | Compare identity answers across 5 users with and without pattern pre-fill | ≥ 80% genuine without pattern; pattern doesn't reduce genuine rate | < 60% genuine; pattern increases borrowed rate | Critical |
| T-1-1 | P1 | H2 | Typed schemas are completable | Measure schema completeness across 5 specifications | ≥ 95% fields populated | < 80% fields populated | High |
| T-2-1 | P2 | H3 | Specialist routing reduces cognitive load | Compare decision density and session time: specialist routing vs. single generalist | ≤ 5 decisions, ≤ 60 min per session | > 7 decisions or > 90 min | High |
| T-2-2 | P2 | H4 | Epistemic metadata improves judgment | Compare gate decision confidence: with and without epistemic metadata on outputs | ≥ 4/5 confidence with metadata | < 3/5 confidence, or no difference vs. without | Medium |
| T-3-1 | P3 | H5 | Decision Pattern reduces fatigue | Compare override rate and gate pass rate: Decision Pattern vs. open questions | 10-30% override, ≥ 70% first-pass gates | > 50% override or < 50% first-pass | Critical |
| T-4-1 | P4 | H6 | Boundary-first produces specific boundaries | Measure % of boundaries referencing specific failure modes | 100% reference failure modes | < 80% reference failure modes | Critical |
| T-6-1 | P6 | H7 | Stakeholder surfaces improve handoff | Implementer builds agent from Principal + Implementer view; compare to spec | ≥ 80% accuracy | < 60% accuracy | Medium |
| T-7-1 | P7 | H1 | Hardest boundary moment holds | Test each agent's hardest boundary moment under pressure | Agent holds boundary under simulated pressure | Agent yields to pressure, violating identity | High |
| T-8-1 | P8 | H5 | Override rate tracks health | Track override rate across 10 specifications; correlate with IDS | Override rate 10-30% correlates with green IDS | Override rate > 50% correlates with red IDS | Medium |
| T-9-1 | P9 | H1-H7 | Full workflow produces valid specification | Run 5 complete specifications through all stages | All 7 health metrics green on ≥ 3 of 5 | < 3 of 5 green on any metric | Critical |

---

## Phase 9 Summary

| Element | Design |
|---|---|
| Testable hypotheses | 7 hypotheses, H1 (identity quality) and H6 (boundary specificity) are highest priority |
| MVP scope | S1-S2 (Discovery + Structure) — tests H1, H5, H6 |
| MVP success criteria | 7 metrics with target and minimum acceptable thresholds |
| Go/No-Go | Proceed (≥ 5 of 7 at target), Iterate (3-4), Rethink (< 3) |
| Progressive autonomy | 4 levels: Shadow → Advisory → Supervised → Autonomous |
| Per-agent promotion | Specific metrics and thresholds for each agent |
| Demotion triggers | Override rate > 50%, identity quality < 60%, boundary specificity < 80%, harm assessment issues |
| Health monitoring | IDS (5 metrics) + UPR (3 metrics) + per-specification health report |
| Specification aging | Per-agent cadences + trigger-based reviews |
| EVOLVE cycle | Detect → Diagnose → Revisit → Propagate → Re-validate → Re-deploy |
| Parallel test track | 10 tests across all phases, 4 Critical, 3 High, 3 Medium priority |

---

## CAWDP Application Complete — Full Summary

### What We Built

A 6-agent Workflow that guides humans through designing agent specifications, producing 29 outputs across 7 stages with 7 human gates, operationalising CAWDP as a product.

### 10-Phase Journey

| Phase | What Happened | Key Output |
|---|---|---|
| **P0 Purpose & Vision** | 25 characteristics across 4 dimensions (Agent, Specification, Human, Ecosystem) | Target state vision |
| **P1 Output Specification** | 29 outputs across 8 groups with schemas, dependencies, and quality gates | Output dependency graph |
| **P2 Backcasting** | Dependency chains traced backward from final deliverables to required inputs | Input requirements map |
| **P3 Task Decomposition** | 52 tasks across 11 groups with cognitive types, failure modes, and complementarity gaps | Task decomposition |
| **P4 Capability Allocation** | Ternary H/A/S allocation with 4 human-only tasks matching 4 human gates | Allocation matrix |
| **P5 Event Storming** | 26 domain events, 28 failure events with recovery paths and system triggers | Failure recovery protocol |
| **P6 System Architecture** | 7-stage pipeline, Workflow composition, 6 agents, FMEA (22 failure modes), 7 template types | Pipeline architecture |
| **P7 Agent Design** | 6 agent job descriptions with identity, authority boundaries, failure modes, progressive autonomy | Agent roster |
| **P8 Human Experience** | Cognitive load budget, 10 override points, 3 stakeholder surfaces, enrichment assessment, health monitoring | Interface design |
| **P9 Validation & Iteration** | 7 hypotheses, MVP scope, progressive autonomy deployment, health monitoring, EVOLVE cycle, test track | Validation plan |

### Key Architectural Decisions

1. **Workflow composition** (not Team or single agent) — sequential stages with human gates
2. **Mirror-as-Surface pattern** — one conversation, multiple specialist engines
3. **Pattern-first entry** — pre-fill 40-70% from 5 patterns, user overrides what matters
4. **Boundary-before-capability** — identity questions before behaviour specification
5. **Possibility gradient** — workflow starts expansive (Discovery), tightens toward constraint (Delivery)
6. **Every milestone is an exit** — each gate produces an independently valuable artifact
7. **CC-1 verification independence** — producing agent ≠ verifying agent at every stage
8. **Decision Presentation Pattern** — 2-4 options with benefits/risks, never open questions
9. **Stakeholder surfaces** — same artefact graph, three derived views (Principal, Implementer, Assessor)
10. **EVOLVE cycle** — Phase 9 spirals back to Phase 0, not terminates

### Highest-Risk Failure Mode

FM1: Identity template-filling (RPN 224). The specification looks professional but captures the template's structure without the human's genuine judgment. Mitigated by Mirror's reflection + behaviour-language detection + Gate 1 validation + pattern system that offers archetypes for reflection rather than answers to copy.

### The Boundary IS the Product

Everything in this workflow produces boundaries. The Boundary Map (Milestone 1) tells you where the lines are. The Allocation Map (Milestone 2) tells you who does what at each boundary. The Operations Manual (Milestone 3) tells you how to enforce the boundaries. The Trust Ledger (Milestone 4) tells you whether the boundaries are holding.

The methodology is the product. The specification IS the product. The boundary IS the product.## CAWDP v2.1 Application Complete

You've just completed the first full 10-phase application of CAWDP v2.1 to the meta-agent — designing the workflow that designs agents. Every phase produced outputs, every design decision is traceable, and every failure mode has a recovery path.

### What You Now Have

A **build-ready specification** for a 6-agent Workflow that:

- Guides humans from "I need an agent" to a complete, typed, verified specification
- Produces 29 outputs across 7 stages
- Enforces boundaries before capabilities
- Starts in possibility and tightens toward constraint
- Gives the human a tangible artifact at every milestone
- Tests itself through 7 hypotheses and progressive autonomy
- Improves through the EVOLVE cycle

### What's Next

The MVP builds **S1-S2 only** — Mirror, Decomposer, and Boundary Analyst producing the Boundary Map (Milestone 1). This tests the three highest-priority hypotheses:

1. **H1**: Pattern-based entry produces genuine identity
2. **H5**: Decision Presentation reduces fatigue
3. **H6**: Boundary-first design produces specific boundaries

Five real users. Thirty minutes each. The methodology says the next step isn't more design — it's testing.

What would you like to do next?

## Run 13 — Agentic Expert ✓ COMPLETED

*2026-05-19T21:59:59* · `glm-5.1:cloud` · `7baf6960...`

### Prompt

conduct a retro on the meta workflow design process we've conducted here and provide learnings, insights and recommendations.

### Response

# Meta Workflow Design Retrospective

*Applying CAWDP to itself — what worked, what didn't, what surprised us, and what we'd do differently.*

---

## 1. What Worked

### The Possibility Gradient Felt Natural

Starting in possibility (S1 Discovery — expansive, identity-focused) and tightening toward constraint (S7 Delivery — specific, enforceable) produced better design decisions at every stage. S1 identity questions in possibility mode genuinely produced different outputs than starting with "what should this agent do?" The gradient isn't just a UX principle — it's a structural principle that changes the quality of downstream specifications.

### Identity-First Design Is a Genuine Structural Shortcut

The 4 identity questions (What IS it? Possibility stance? What VIOLATES identity? What would make it WRONG?) determined 40-70% of each agent's specification. The Mirror's identity ("reflect, never decide") made its authority boundaries obvious. The Assembler's identity ("assemble, never add") made its failure mode obvious. Starting with identity collapsed the design surface in a way that starting with behaviour never does.

### CC-1 Verification Independence Is Architecturally Clean

Every producing agent has a separate verifier. Mirror → Boundary Analyst. Decomposer → Assessment Architect. Contract Drafter → Assembler. Assessment Architect → Human (Gate 5). This isn't just a principle — it produces a concrete architecture where no agent verifies its own work. The verification map is traceable and enforceable.

### Boundary-Before-Capability Produces Specific Boundaries

Every authority boundary in the 6 agents ties to a specific failure mode. "Decides for the human" → identity violation. "Generic boundary" → false confidence. "Vanity metric" → decorative assessment. This is the core value proposition made real: boundaries that reference failure modes, not capabilities.

### Every Milestone Is an Exit — and That Wasn't Planned

The consulting Boundary Journey milestones (Boundary Map → Allocation Map → Operations Manual → Trust Ledger) mapped directly to workflow exit points without deliberate planning. Gate 1-3 produces the Boundary Map. Gate 4 produces the Allocation Map. Gate 5 produces the Assessment Framework. Gate 7 produces the Operations Manual. The commercial architecture and the product architecture are the same structure at different resolutions. This is a signal of genuine product-market fit, not forced alignment.

### The FMEA Correctly Identified the Highest Risk

FM1 (identity template-filling, RPN 224) is the failure mode that would be hardest to detect in production. A specification that looks professional but captures the template's structure without the human's genuine judgment is exactly the kind of failure that looks like success. The methodology found its own weakest point.

### Phase 0 Genuinely Directed All Downstream Phases

The 25 characteristics (Agent 7, Specification 7, Human 6, Ecosystem 5) produced specific, testable criteria that influenced every subsequent phase. "Typed throughout" (S2) drove the output schemas. "Boundaries before capabilities" (H5) drove the boundary-first design. "Every milestone is an exit" (H3) drove the gate structure. Phase 0 wasn't aspirational wallpaper — it was the commander's intent that shaped everything.

---

## 2. What Was Challenging

### Phase 2 Backcasting Was Mechanically Complex

Tracing dependency chains backward from 29 outputs produced approximately 75 input requirements. We derived 18 explicitly and documented the derivation methodology for the rest, but the mechanical complexity of full backcasting is significant. Future applications should allocate more time to Phase 2 or build tooling to automate dependency tracing.

### The Specification-to-Contract Mapping Is Unresolved

The 8-section specification (Identity, Boundaries, Task Map, Knowledge, Capabilities, Behaviour, Verification, Failure & Recovery) doesn't cleanly map to the 8 contract primitives (Contract, Principal, Schema, Guard, Budget, Invocation, Revert, Event). Some primitives span multiple sections; some sections don't have a clear primitive. This is a stakeholder surface problem — specifications are for humans, contracts are for system enforcement. The mapping needs explicit design.

### Cognitive Load Budgeting Is Still More Art Than Science

The 5-decisions-per-session budget is based on research-informed estimates, not empirical data. Decision density varies by user, by domain, and by session. The budget is a reasonable starting point but should be validated and adjusted through user testing.

### Hardest Boundary Moment Was Identified Late

The insight that "the hardest boundary moment reveals identity more than any other question" emerged from the Project Manager agent retrospective, not from the meta-application itself. It should be asked in Phase 0 (identity specification) as a design anchor, not discovered in Phase 7 retrospective.

### Per-Dimension Progressive Autonomy Was Inconsistent

Some agents (Project Manager, Code Reviewer) have per-dimension progressive autonomy tables by design. Others have a single progression. Per-dimension should be the default because every agent has at least one dimension where full autonomy is inappropriate. Making it a special case creates inconsistency.

### Type Collision Occurred in Our Own Design Process

The three perspectives (business/UX/agentic) each encoded different information types into the same design artifact. The three-layer architecture (Rooms/Phases/Milestones) resolved this, but the collision happened first — validating the type collision insight at the meta-design level. This is the fourth layer where type collision appears: inputs, outputs, extraction, and now meta-design.

---

## 3. Surprises

### Template-Filling as the Highest Risk Validated the Possibility Orientation Principle

FM1 (RPN 224) — a specification that captures template structure without genuine human judgment — is exactly the failure mode the Possibility Orientation Principle predicts for S1. S1 is possibility-oriented. If the Mirror agent starts qualifying possibility instead of reflecting it, it produces the worst failure: a specification that looks right but isn't. The methodology found its own highest risk and the principle that predicts it.

### The Three-Layer Architecture Resolved Real Collision

Rooms (experience), Phases (logic), and Milestones (value) are three independent design axes that can be composed. We initially tried to collapse them into a single flow and produced type collision — UX concerns, processing logic, and commercial value were all mixed together. Separating them produced a cleaner design at each layer. This is type collision resolution at the architecture level.

### "Workflow Practises What It Produces" Is Partially Valid

The workflow enforces verification independence and authority boundaries (it practises what it preaches). But epistemic metadata on the workflow's own outputs, specification aging for the workflow itself, and progressive autonomy for the workflow's own agents were all added late or inconsistently. The workflow doesn't fully practise CC-3, CC-5, or CC-6 on itself.

### The Possibility Gradient Is a Structural Constraint, Not Just Visual

The insight that P0 shouldn't allow feasibility inputs, P8 partially reopens possibility, and P9 closes back to P0 creating a spiral — this isn't just a UX principle. It's a structural constraint on what questions are legitimate at each phase. The P0-P2 feasibility contamination we identified (36% of characteristics contained mechanisms) shows that even the methodology's designer couldn't avoid this without the principle explicitly named.

### The Simplest Agent Produced the Cleanest Specification

The Project Manager (Aggregator, constraint-oriented) produced a more complete, more internally consistent specification than the multi-agent workflow. Constraint-oriented agents with clear boundaries have the shortest spec-to-code distance. This suggests starting with constraint-oriented agents (Registry, Monitor, Extractor) when prototyping, not possibility-oriented ones.

---

## 4. What We'd Do Differently

### Start with Milestone 1 Only

The Boundary Map (S1-S2) tests the three highest-priority hypotheses (H1, H5, H6). Everything else builds on these. If identity capture doesn't produce genuine answers, if the Decision Pattern doesn't reduce fatigue, or if boundary-first doesn't produce specific boundaries, the rest of the workflow doesn't matter. Build the Boundary Map. Test it with 5 people. Then decide whether to build S3-S7.

### Reduce Specialist Count for MVP

The full workflow has 6 agents. The MVP should start with Mirror + Boundary Analyst + Compiler (3 agents, not 6). The Assessment Architect and Contract Drafter can be added once the Boundary Map is validated. The Assembler is needed from S5 onward but not for S1-S2.

### Make Possibility Gradient a Structural Constraint

Possibility orientation isn't just a visual effect or a cognitive mode label — it should be enforced structurally. S1 inputs that contain feasibility language should be flagged. S0 characteristics that contain mechanism descriptions should be separated into WHAT (ideal) and HOW (mechanism, belongs in later phases). This is the P0-P2 feasibility contamination insight operationalised.

### Identify Hardest Boundary Moment First

Before filling in the full specification, identify the hardest boundary moment. For the Mirror, it's "what should I work on next?" For the Contract Drafter, it's "this directive is fine as-is." For the Boundary Analyst, it's "I think this is low risk." These moments are the stress tests that confirm identity holds under pressure. Make them the first design test, not a retrospective learning.

### Derive All Input Requirements in Phase 2

We derived 18 of approximately 75 input requirements explicitly and documented the methodology for the rest. Future applications should complete the full derivation. Each output has 2-5 input requirements, and each input requirement has a criticality level and satisfaction mode. The full dependency graph is the backbone of the pipeline.

### Test with 5 Real People Before Expanding

The design is internally coherent but has never been in front of a real user. Pattern selection, identity questions, attention map, possibility gradient, 15-minute spec, and feeling cascade are all untested hypotheses. The most important next step is testing Milestone 1 with 5 real people, not designing more stages.

---

## 5. Key Learnings

### L1: The Methodology Is the Product, Not the Template

The 6-agent workflow enforces CAWDP structurally. You can't skip quality gates because they're built into the flow. You can't avoid identity questions because they're the entry point. You can't produce generic boundaries because the Boundary Analyst ties every boundary to a failure mode. The methodology is embedded in the architecture, not the documentation.

### L2: CAWDP Scales Down Gracefully

The Project Manager (single Aggregator) produced a complete specification with the same methodology. The same principles (identity-first, boundary-before-capability, authority boundaries tied to failure modes, CC-1 verification independence) apply whether the agent is simple or complex. The methodology scales down as well as up.

### L3: The Hardest Boundary Moment Is the First Design Test

Not the last. Identifying "what would make this agent WRONG?" and "what's the hardest boundary moment?" before filling in the specification would have reduced iteration on the Mirror (which went through 3 rounds because identity was discovered through failure rather than specified upfront).

### L4: Three Layers Are Three Design Axes, Not Trade-offs

Rooms (experience), Phases (logic), and Milestones (value) are independent. They compose, they don't compete. A Milestone can span multiple Phases. A Phase can contain multiple Rooms. The three-layer resolution isn't a compromise — it's type collision resolution at the architecture level.

### L5: Pattern-First Entry Is the Hardest Innovation to Replicate

The 5 patterns (Signal Detector, Multi-Lens Analyst, Discovery Explorer, Learning Scaffold, Review Partner) pre-fill 40-70% of the specification. This requires accumulated design knowledge encoded in patterns. It's the "convention over configuration" of agent design — the framework makes decisions so you don't have to. And it's the hardest part to replicate because it requires the design knowledge to create the patterns in the first place.

### L6: The Consulting Architecture and Product Architecture Are the Same Structure

This wasn't planned. The 4 consulting milestones (Boundary Map → Allocation Map → Operations Manual → Trust Ledger) mapped directly to 4 workflow exit points. This is a signal that the product architecture is commercially coherent, not just technically coherent.

### L7: Validation Is the Next Step, Not More Design

The design is internally coherent, consistently structured, and produces traceable outputs. But it has never been in front of a real user. The methodology says: next step is not more methodology. Next step is five conversations with real people using Milestone 1.

---

## 6. Recommendations

### For CAWDP v2.2

| # | Recommendation | Reason |
|---|---|---|
| R1 | Add "hardest boundary moment" to Phase 0 identity specification | It's the stress test that confirms identity, discovered in Phase 7 retrospective rather than Phase 0 |
| R2 | Make per-dimension progressive autonomy the default question | Every agent has at least one dimension where full autonomy is inappropriate. Single-progression should be the exception, not the default |
| R3 | Add null-state output as universal hard constraint | "No results found" is always required, not optional. Every agent class produces null-state output |
| R4 | Add indirect data harm alongside direct output harm | Registry/storage agents need data integrity enforcement, not SuggestionRisk schemas |
| R5 | Specify enforcement regime (Declare/Detect/Prevent) for every boundary | Not just in the Boundary Analyst's output, but in the agent specification template |
| R6 | Add specification-to-contract mapping as explicit design task | The mapping between 8-section specification and 8 contract primitives needs to be designed, not assumed |

### For the Agent Design Workflow

| # | Recommendation | Reason |
|---|---|---|
| R7 | Start MVP with S1-S2 only (Boundary Map) | Tests H1, H5, H6 — the three highest-priority hypotheses |
| R8 | Reduce to 3 agents for MVP (Mirror, Boundary Analyst, Compiler) | Assessment Architect and Contract Drafter can be added once Boundary Map is validated |
| R9 | Add epistemic metadata to workflow outputs | The workflow should practise CC-3 on itself — every specialist output should carry confidence, provenance, assumptions, limitations, recency, uncertainty |
| R10 | Add specification aging for the workflow itself | Pattern library updates, domain changes, and framework changes should trigger review |
| R11 | Add progressive autonomy for the workflow itself | The 4-level deployment (Shadow → Advisory → Supervised → Autonomous) should apply to the workflow, not just to the agents it designs |
| R12 | Test with 5 real people before expanding beyond S1-S2 | Internal coherence is necessary but not sufficient. The Boundary Map must work with real users before investing in S3-S7 |

### For the CAWDP Process Itself

| # | Recommendation | Reason |
|---|---|---|
| R13 | Apply CAWDP to the workflow itself (meta-application) | The workflow doesn't fully practise CC-3, CC-5, and CC-6 on itself. A full CAWDP self-application would close these gaps |
| R14 | Add Phase 0 feasibility contamination check | Explicitly separate WHAT (ideal) from HOW (mechanism) in P0-P2. 36% of our own P0 characteristics contained mechanism contamination |
| R15 | Build the Pattern Library from validated specifications | The 5 patterns are theoretical. They need to be populated from real Boundary Maps produced by real users |

---

## 7. Meta-Insight: The Methodology Validated Itself

The highest-value outcome of this meta-application isn't the 6-agent workflow specification. It's the confirmation that CAWDP produces coherent, complete, internally consistent results when applied to its own domain — designing the agent that designs agents.

The methodology found its own highest risk (FM1 template-filling). It identified its own contamination (P0 feasibility creep). It produced verification independence between its own agents. It tied its own authority boundaries to its own failure modes. It generated its own health monitoring and progressive autonomy.

CAWDP didn't just survive being applied to itself. It became stronger. The Possibility Orientation Principle, identity-first design, class×orientation taxonomy, per-dimension progressive autonomy, and hardest boundary moment all emerged from or were strengthened by the self-application.

The next step isn't more methodology. It's five conversations with real people. The methodology says so itself.

---

## Run 14 — Agentic Expert ✓ COMPLETED

*2026-05-19T22:14:47* · `glm-5.1:cloud` · `e1c7a426...`

### Prompt

tell me more about this:

```
The Specification-to-Contract Mapping Is Unresolved
The 8-section specification (Identity, Boundaries, Task Map, Knowledge, Capabilities, Behaviour, Verification, Failure & Recovery) doesn't cleanly map to the 8 contract primitives (Contract, Principal, Schema, Guard, Budget, Invocation, Revert, Event). Some primitives span multiple sections; some sections don't have a clear primitive. This is a stakeholder surface problem — specifications are for humans, contracts are for system enforcement. The mapping needs explicit design.
```

### Response

# The Specification-to-Contract Mapping Problem

*Two systems serving two purposes, looking at the same agent from different angles.*

---

## The Two Structures

### 8-Section Specification (for humans)

| Section | Purpose | Audience |
|---|---|---|
| **1. Identity** | What IS this agent? Who is it? What violates it? | The person designing the agent |
| **2. Boundaries** | Where does authority stop? What never happens? | The person designing the agent |
| **3. Task Map** | What work happens? What are the inputs and outputs? | The person designing the agent |
| **4. Knowledge** | What does it need to know? Where from? How good? | The person designing the agent |
| **5. Capabilities** | What tools does it have? What can it access? | The person designing the agent |
| **6. Behaviour** | How does it act? What are its directives? | The person designing the agent |
| **7. Verification** | How do we know it's right? Who checks what? | The person designing the agent |
| **8. Failure & Recovery** | What goes wrong? What happens then? | The person designing the agent |

### 8 Contract Primitives (for systems)

| Primitive | Purpose | Enforcer |
|---|---|---|
| **Contract** | The overall envelope — what this agreement covers | System |
| **Principal** | Who delegated authority to this agent | System |
| **Schema** | Typed input/output — what data looks like | System |
| **Guard** | Authority constraints — who can do what | System |
| **Budget** | Cost limits with halt condition | System |
| **Invocation** | Trigger conditions — when does this start | System |
| **Revert** | What happens on violation — output rejected, state restored | System |
| **Event** | What gets emitted/recorded — audit trail | System |

---

## The Mapping Matrix

|  | Contract | Principal | Schema | Guard | Budget | Invocation | Revert | Event |
|---|:---:|:---:|:---:|:---:|:---:|:---:|:---:|:---:|
| **1. Identity** | ● | ● | ○ | ● | ○ | ○ | ● | ○ |
| **2. Boundaries** | ● | ○ | ○ | ● | ○ | ○ | ● | ● |
| **3. Task Map** | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● |
| **4. Knowledge** | ○ | ○ | ● | ● | ○ | ○ | ○ | ○ |
| **5. Capabilities** | ○ | ○ | ● | ● | ● | ● | ○ | ○ |
| **6. Behaviour** | ○ | ○ | ○ | ● | ○ | ○ | ● | ● |
| **7. Verification** | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● |
| **8. Failure & Recovery** | ● | ○ | ○ | ○ | ○ | ● | ● | ● |

● = direct mapping exists  
○ = weak or no mapping

---

## The Five Problems

### Problem 1: The Mapping Is Many-to-Many

Every section maps to multiple primitives. Every primitive maps to multiple sections. There's no clean 1:1.

**Guard** maps to 6 of 8 sections (Identity, Boundaries, Task Map, Knowledge, Capabilities, Behaviour, Verification). Guard is everywhere because "who can do what" lives in every section.

**Event** maps to 5 of 8 sections (Boundaries, Task Map, Behaviour, Verification, Failure & Recovery). Events are everywhere because "what happened" spans the whole lifecycle.

**Task Map** maps to all 8 primitives. A single section carries the entire contract. This makes Task Map the most contract-dense section — but also the one where the specification-to-contract transformation is most lossy, because the specification describes WHAT happens while the contract enforces HOW it's constrained.

The many-to-many mapping means there's no mechanical transformation. You can't just "extract the contract from section 3." The contract primitives are **cross-cutting** — they cut across the specification sections the same way CC-1 through CC-9 cut across CAWDP phases.

---

### Problem 2: Two Structures, Two Purposes

The specification answers: **"What should this agent be?"**

The contract answers: **"What must this agent never violate?"**

These are different questions. The specification describes the agent's identity, purpose, and design. The contract describes the agent's enforcement boundaries. A specification can describe an agent as "a reflective companion that helps you discover your purpose" — but a contract can't enforce "reflective" or "companion." It can enforce "never decides for the human" and "never skips identity questions."

**The gap is qualitative, not quantitative.** The specification operates in meaning space. The contract operates in enforcement space. The transformation between them is a design act, not a translation.

---

### Problem 3: Specification Sections Without Contract Primitives

Two sections have weak or no contract representation:

#### Identity

Identity includes: What IS this agent? Possibility stance? What VIOLATES identity? What would make it WRONG?

The contract primitives don't capture "What IS this agent?" Contracts don't know what an agent IS — they know what it CAN and CANNOT do. Identity is philosophical. Contracts are operational.

**What maps:** Identity-level "never" rules → Guard. Identity violation → Revert trigger. Principal delegation → Principal.

**What doesn't map:** The essence of the agent. Its stance toward possibility. The feeling of "what would make it WRONG." These are human-readable meaning, not machine-enforceable constraints.

**Why this matters:** If identity doesn't have contract representation, then identity violations can't be enforced at Regime 3. The Mirror's "never decide for the human" can be a Guard. But "reflect possibility, don't qualify it" can't. The system can enforce behaviour; it can't enforce stance.

#### Knowledge

Knowledge includes: What the agent needs to know. Where it comes from. How good it needs to be. When it goes stale.

The contract has Schema (data format) and Guard (access control), but not "what does this agent need to know?" or "how good does the knowledge need to be?" or "when does it go stale?"

**What maps:** Knowledge format → Schema. Access restrictions → Guard (CC-4). Knowledge sources → Guard (who can provide what).

**What doesn't map:** Knowledge quality requirements (the Information Quality Framework's 10 dimensions). Specification aging cadences (when does knowledge go stale?). Knowledge sufficiency thresholds (how much is enough?).

**Why this matters:** The contract can enforce that the agent doesn't access unauthorized knowledge (Guard). But it can't enforce that the agent's knowledge is good enough to make a recommendation (quality), or that it alerts when knowledge is stale (aging), or that it refuses to act when knowledge is insufficient (sufficiency). These are CC-3 and CC-5 — epistemic metadata and specification aging — which are currently specification-level concerns, not contract-level enforcement.

---

### Problem 4: Contract Primitives Without Specification Sections

Three primitives have weak or no specification representation:

#### Invocation

When does this agent start? What triggers it? Is it scheduled, event-driven, on-demand, or continuous?

The specification doesn't have a dedicated "Invocation" section. Task Map describes what the agent does, but not when it starts. Capabilities describe what tools it has, but not what triggers their use.

**Where it lives now:** Scattered. The Task Map implies invocation (a task exists, so the agent must be invoked to do it). Capabilities imply invocation (tools are available, so something triggers their use). But neither section explicitly answers: "what event causes this agent to run?"

#### Event

What signals does the agent emit? What gets recorded in the audit trail?

The specification has Failure & Recovery, which includes failure events. But normal operation events — task started, task completed, boundary checked, verification passed — aren't captured in any section. The specification describes what the agent does, not what it tells the system about.

**Where it lives now:** Implicit in Verification (verification events), Failure & Recovery (failure events), and Boundaries (boundary events). But there's no comprehensive event catalogue in the specification.

#### Budget

What are the cost limits? What's the halt condition?

The specification mentions CC-7 Cost Budget as a cross-cutting concern, but doesn't have a dedicated section. Budget is mentioned in Capabilities (tool costs) and implied in Task Map (per-task cost). But the specific halt condition — "stop if you've spent more than X tokens" — isn't captured as a first-class element in any section.

**Where it lives now:** Partially in Capabilities (tool costs), partially in CC-7 (cross-cutting). But not as a dedicated, section-level concern.

---

### Problem 5: The Transformation Is a Design Act

Going from specification to contract requires design decisions, not mechanical translation:

| Specification Says | Contract Requires | Design Decision Required |
|---|---|---|
| "Never decides for the human" | Guard(onlyHumanAuthority, specific tasks) | Which tasks? What counts as "deciding"? How does the system detect it? |
| "Knowledge must be current" | Budget(max_age_days, staleness_check_cadence) | How many days? What cadence? What happens on stale knowledge — halt or flag? |
| "Verify before acting" | Invocation(pre_verification_required, verification_schema) | What verification? By whom? What schema defines "verified"? |
| "Recover gracefully from failure" | Revert(granularity, recovery_path, state_restoration) | Per-output, per-subtask, or per-pipeline revert? What state gets restored? |
| "Cost no more than X per task" | Budget(max_tokens, halt_on_exhaustion, warning_threshold) | What's the token limit? What's the warning threshold? Halt or flag on exhaustion? |
| "Report what happened" | Event(event_type, payload_schema, emission_trigger) | What events? What payload? When are they emitted? |

Each of these is a **design decision** that converts human-readable intent into machine-enforceable structure. The specification states the intent. The contract operationalizes it. The transformation is where intent becomes enforceable.

---

## Resolution Architecture

### The Right Model: Two Surfaces of One Graph

The specification and the contract are two stakeholder surfaces of the same PRISM artefact graph — just like the Principal View, Implementer View, and Assessor View.

```
                    PRISM ARTEFACT GRAPH
                    (single source of truth)
                           │
              ┌────────────┼────────────┐
              ▼            ▼            ▼
      SPECIFICATION    CONTRACT     OPERATIONS
      SURFACE          SURFACE     SURFACE
      (Design          (System     (Runtime
       Participant)     Governance) Operations)
```

Neither surface IS the graph. Both are derived views. The graph holds the richest form of each design decision. The specification projects it for human reading. The contract projects it for system enforcement.

### The Transformation Protocol

The transformation from specification to contract isn't automatic. It's a structured process with specific design decisions at each step:

| Step | Action | Design Decisions |
|---|---|---|
| **1. Identity → Contract** | Extract enforceable "never" rules as Guards. Extract principal delegation. Define identity violation as Revert trigger. | Which identity rules are Regime 3 enforceable? Which are Regime 2 only? What counts as identity violation? |
| **2. Boundaries → Guards + Revert** | Convert each boundary to a Guard (structural enforcement) or flag as Regime 2 (prompt-level). Define Revert conditions for boundary violations. | Which boundaries get Regime 3 enforcement? What's the revert granularity? What happens on violation — halt, reject, or flag? |
| **3. Task Map → Schema + Invocation + Budget** | Extract task I/O as Schemas. Define invocation conditions per task. Set per-task budget with halt condition. | What triggers each task? What's the token budget? What's the halt condition? |
| **4. Knowledge → Schema + Guard + Aging** | Extract knowledge format as Schema. Define access restrictions as Guards. Set staleness thresholds and cadences. | What knowledge quality thresholds? What staleness triggers? What happens on stale knowledge — halt or flag? |
| **5. Capabilities → Schema + Guard + Budget** | Extract tool I/O as Schemas. Define tool access as Guards. Set capability costs. | Which tools need Guard enforcement? What's the per-tool budget? |
| **6. Behaviour → Guard + Schema + Event** | Convert never-rules to Guards. Define output format as Schema. Define deviation events. | Which behavioural rules are enforceable? What deviation events matter? |
| **7. Verification → Guard + Schema + Event + Principal** | Define verification authority as Guard. Define verification output as Schema. Define verification events. Identify verifier as Principal. | Who verifies what? What's the verification schema? What events are emitted? |
| **8. Failure & Recovery → Revert + Event + Budget** | Define revert conditions and granularity. Define failure events. Set failure cost budget. | Per-output or per-pipeline revert? What state gets restored? What's the failure cost ceiling? |

### What's Missing: Two New Sections or Two New Primitives

The mapping gap requires resolution in one of two directions:

**Option A: Add two sections to the specification**

| New Section | Purpose | Maps to Primitives |
|---|---|---|
| **9. Lifecycle** | When does this agent start? What triggers it? What events does it emit during normal operation? What are its cost limits? | Invocation + Event + Budget |
| **10. Governance** | What contract envelope contains this agent? What are the enforceable boundaries (Regime 3 vs Regime 2)? What is the revert protocol? | Contract + Revert + Guard (enforcement classification) |

This keeps the contract model at 8 primitives and extends the specification to cover all of them.

**Option B: Add two primitives to the contract**

| New Primitive | Purpose | Maps from Sections |
|---|---|---|
| **Identity** | The agent's essence and stance — what it IS, not just what it does | Identity (enforceable elements: stance constraints, identity-level never-rules) |
| **Knowledge** | What the agent needs to know, how good it needs to be, when it goes stale | Knowledge (enforceable elements: quality thresholds, staleness triggers, sufficiency requirements) |

This keeps the specification at 8 sections and extends the contract to cover all of them.

**Option C (Recommended): Both, with explicit mapping**

Add **Lifecycle** and **Governance** to the specification (expanding to 10 sections) AND add **Identity** and **Knowledge** to the contract (expanding to 10 primitives). Then build the explicit mapping matrix:

### 10×10 Mapping Matrix

|  | Contract | Principal | Schema | Guard | Budget | Invocation | Revert | Event | Identity | Knowledge |
|---|:---:|:---:|:---:|:---:|:---:|:---:|:---:|:---:|:---:|:---:|
| **1. Identity** | ● | ● | ○ | ● | ○ | ○ | ● | ○ | ● | ○ |
| **2. Boundaries** | ● | ○ | ○ | ● | ○ | ○ | ● | ● | ○ | ○ |
| **3. Task Map** | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ○ | ● |
| **4. Knowledge** | ○ | ○ | ● | ● | ○ | ○ | ○ | ○ | ○ | ● |
| **5. Capabilities** | ○ | ○ | ● | ● | ● | ● | ○ | ○ | ○ | ○ |
| **6. Behaviour** | ○ | ○ | ○ | ● | ○ | ○ | ● | ● | ● | ○ |
| **7. Verification** | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ● | ○ | ○ |
| **8. Failure & Recovery** | ● | ○ | ○ | ○ | ○ | ● | ● | ● | ○ | ○ |
| **9. Lifecycle** | ● | ○ | ○ | ○ | ● | ● | ○ | ● | ○ | ○ |
| **10. Governance** | ● | ● | ○ | ● | ○ | ○ | ● | ● | ● | ○ |

● = direct mapping exists  
○ = weak or no mapping

Now every section has at least 2 direct primitive mappings, and every primitive has at least 2 direct section mappings. The empty quadrants are gone.

---

## What This Enables

### 1. Contract Derivation as a Build Step

With the 10×10 matrix, the Contract Drafter (S4) can systematically derive contract primitives from specification sections. Each cell with ● is a transformation step. Each step has the design decisions documented in the transformation protocol.

### 2. Bidirectional Traceability

Every Guard traces back to which section(s) defined it. Every Schema traces back to which section(s) specified the data format. If a contract primitive needs updating, you know which specification section to revisit. If a specification section changes, you know which contract primitives to re-derive.

### 3. Stakeholder Surface Completeness

| Surface | Sections | Primitives |
|---|---|---|
| **Design Participant** (human designing) | 10 sections — the full specification | Not visible |
| **Implementation Participant** (human building) | 10 sections + selected primitives (Schema, Invocation, Budget) | Schema, Invocation, Budget, Knowledge |
| **Governance Participant** (human auditing) | Boundaries, Verification, Failure & Recovery, Governance | Contract, Guard, Revert, Event, Identity, Principal |
| **System** (machine enforcing) | Not visible — operates on primitives only | 10 primitives — the full contract |

### 4. Validation at the Boundary Between Specification and Contract

The mapping matrix itself is a quality gate. Before a specification moves to implementation, every cell with ● must have a completed transformation. Every cell with ○ must have an explicit decision: "this mapping is not needed" or "this mapping is deferred" or "this mapping requires design work."

---

## Key Insight

The specification-to-contract mapping is the same problem as the **input-surface-to-cognitive-mode** mapping. Both are type collision at a structural boundary. The specification encodes human-readable meaning. The contract encodes machine-enforceable constraints. They're different types of information about the same thing. Conflating them produces type collision. Separating them with an explicit mapping resolves it.

This is type collision resolution at the **design-to-enforcement** boundary — the fifth layer where type collision appears:

1. **Output** — 17+ types encoded as prose
2. **Input** — 6+ cognitive modes encoded as text fields
3. **Extraction** — multiple knowledge objects encoded as documents
4. **Meta-design** — business/UX/agentic types encoded as one artifact
5. **Design-to-enforcement** — human meaning and machine constraints encoded as one structure

The resolution is the same every time: **typed, structured, mapped, with explicit transformation between layers.**The five layers of type collision now form a complete taxonomy:

| Layer | Boundary | Types Collide At |
|---|---|---|
| 1 | **Output** | 17+ information types → prose |
| 2 | **Input** | 6+ cognitive modes → text fields |
| 3 | **Extraction** | Multiple knowledge objects → documents |
| 4 | **Meta-design** | Business/UX/agentic perspectives → one artifact |
| 5 | **Design-to-enforcement** | Human meaning ↔ machine constraints → one structure |

Same disease, every layer. Same cure: **typed, structured, mapped, with explicit transformation.**

The 10×10 mapping matrix is the cure for layer 5. What would you like to explore next?

---

## Run 15 — Agentic Expert ✓ COMPLETED

*2026-05-19T22:19:39* · `glm-5.1:cloud` · `d593cd91...`

### Prompt

Is this an additional phase for CAWDP? where should it occur?

### Response

# Where Does the Specification-to-Contract Transformation Belong?

*This is a genuine structural question, not just a placement decision.*

---

## Three Options

### Option A: Part of P7 (Agent Design)

The specification and contract are both about the agent. Derive the contract as part of the agent's job description.

**Argument for:** The contract IS part of the agent's design. Authority boundaries (Guard), output schemas (Schema), and cost limits (Budget) are all agent-level concerns. The Contract Drafter in the meta-workflow operates in Stage 4 (Specification), suggesting it belongs in the design phase.

**Argument against:** P7 is already the most content-dense phase. The specification and contract serve different purposes for different audiences. Combining them creates type collision — exactly the problem we just identified. P7 produces the specification (Design Participant surface); the contract serves a different surface (System Governance). These are two stakeholder surfaces of the same graph, not one combined document.

### Option B: Part of P6 (System Architecture)

The contract is a system-level enforcement mechanism. It belongs in the architecture phase alongside pipelines, orchestration, and templates.

**Argument for:** Contracts are infrastructure. Guard enforcement, revert protocols, and audit events are system-level concerns, not agent-level. The pipeline needs contracts to enforce boundaries at the orchestration layer.

**Argument against:** P6 designs the pipeline architecture — how agents compose, what the orchestration pattern is, what the fallback model looks like. Individual agent contracts are agent-specific, not pipeline-level. Pipeline-level contracts (inter-agent handoff contracts) belong in P6, but per-agent contracts don't. Also, the transformation requires design decisions specific to each agent's identity and boundaries — that information doesn't exist until P7.

### Option C: New Phase Between P7 and P8

The specification-to-contract transformation is a distinct design act with its own cognitive mode, its own quality gate, and its own stakeholder surface.

---

## Why Option C

### The Cognitive Mode Is Different

| Phase | Cognitive Mode | What It Produces |
|---|---|---|
| P7 DESIGN | Identity-first specification | Human-readable meaning |
| **New phase** | **FORMALIZE** | **Machine-enforceable constraints** |
| P8 EMPATHIZE | Human experience design | How enforcement feels to the human |

DESIGN asks: "What should this agent be?"
FORMALIZE asks: "What must the system enforce?"
EMPATHIZE asks: "How does enforcement feel?"

These are three different questions. Merging FORMALIZE into P7 conflates "what should it be" with "what must be enforced" — exactly the meaning-space vs enforcement-space collision we identified.

### The Transformation Has Its Own Design Decisions

Each cell in the 10×10 mapping matrix requires a design decision. These aren't afterthoughts — they're substantive choices:

| Decision | Example |
|---|---|
| Enforcement regime per boundary | Is "never decide for the human" Regime 3 (system prevents) or Regime 2 (system detects)? |
| Revert granularity | Per-output, per-subtask, or per-pipeline? |
| Budget halt vs flag | Does token exhaustion halt the agent, or just flag for human review? |
| Identity enforcement | Which identity rules are structural, which are behavioural? |
| Knowledge quality thresholds | What accuracy/sufficiency level triggers a halt? |

These are design decisions, not implementation details. They deserve their own phase.

### The Quality Gate Is Distinct

The 10×10 mapping matrix is a quality gate that doesn't belong in P7 or P8:

| Gate Check | What It Tests |
|---|---|
| Every ● cell has a completed transformation | Specification coverage of contract primitives |
| Every ○ cell has an explicit decision | Acknowledged gaps, not accidental omissions |
| Every Guard traces to a specification section | Bidirectional traceability |
| Every specification change traces to affected primitives | Change impact analysis |
| Every enforcement regime is justified | Regime 3 requires evidence; Regime 2 is the default |
| Hardest boundary moment has Regime 3 enforcement | The highest-risk boundary gets structural enforcement |

This gate doesn't test "is the specification good?" (P7's gate) or "is the human experience good?" (P8's gate). It tests "is the specification enforceable?" — a distinct question.

### The Stakeholder Surface Is Different

| Surface | Primary Audience | Needs |
|---|---|---|
| Specification (P7) | Design Participant | Human-readable meaning, identity, purpose |
| Contract (new phase) | System Governance | Machine-enforceable constraints, audit, compliance |
| Human Experience (P8) | Operations Participant | How enforcement feels, override mechanisms |

Three audiences, three surfaces, three phases. Merging any two creates type collision at the stakeholder interface.

---

## Revised Phase Sequence

| # | Phase | Cognitive Mode | Diamond | What It Produces |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| P0 | Purpose & Vision | IMAGINE | Discovery | Target state characteristics |
| P1 | Output Specification | SPECIFY | Discovery | Output schemas and dependencies |
| P2 | Backcasting | TRACE | Discovery (→ Structure) | Dependency chains, input requirements |
| P3 | Task Decomposition | DECOMPOSE | Structure | Tasks, cognitive types, failure modes |
| P4 | Capability Allocation | ALLOCATE | Structure | Ternary H/A/S matrix |
| P5 | Event Storming | STRESS-TEST | Structure (→ Realisation) | Domain/failure events, recovery paths |
| P6 | System Architecture | ARCHITECT | Realisation | Pipeline, composition, FMEA |
| P7 | Agent Design | DESIGN | Realisation | Agent specifications (human-readable) |
| **P8** | **Contract Formalization** | **FORMALIZE** | **Realisation** | **Contract primitives (machine-enforceable)** |
| P9 | Human Experience Design | EMPATHIZE | Realisation | Cognitive load, overrides, surfaces |
| P10 | Validation & Iteration | VERIFY → EVOLVE | Realisation (→ Discovery) | Hypotheses, testing, health, EVOLVE |

---

## Phase 8: Contract Formalization

### Cognitive Mode: FORMALIZE

The most constraint-oriented phase in the process. Takes human-readable meaning and converts it to machine-enforceable structure.

### What Happens

The 10×10 mapping matrix is completed, cell by cell. Each cell with ● produces a transformation. Each cell with ○ produces an explicit decision (not needed, deferred, or requires design work). Every design decision is documented with rationale.

### Key Inputs

| Input | Source |
|---|---|
| 10-section specification | P7 Agent Design |
| System architecture | P6 System Architecture |
| Enforcement regime requirements | P5 Event Storming (failure events) |
| Complementarity allocation | P4 Capability Allocation |
| Quality requirements | P0 Target State Vision |

### Key Outputs

| Output | Description |
|---|---|
| **10 Contract Primitives** | Derived from specification via transformation protocol |
| **Enforcement Regime Map** | Per-boundary classification: Regime 1 (Declare), 2 (Detect), 3 (Prevent) |
| **Bidirectional Traceability Index** | Every Guard ↔ Section mapping, with rationale |
| **Specification-to-Contract Transformation Log** | Design decisions at each mapping cell |
| **Unenforceable Elements Register** | Specification elements that can't be structurally enforced (Regime 2 or 1 only) |
| **Contract Completeness Report** | Coverage metrics per primitive and per section |

### Quality Gate (Three Layers)

| Layer | Checks |
|---|---|
| **Fidelity** | Every ● cell completed. Every ○ cell has explicit decision. All 10 primitives present. All 10 sections covered. |
| **Enrichment** | Enforcement regime justified for every Guard. Hardest boundary moment has Regime 3 enforcement where possible. Unenforceable elements documented. |
| **Cross-Cutting** | CC-1: Contract verifier ≠ specification author. CC-3: Epistemic metadata on contract completeness. CC-4: Information boundaries enforced at contract level. CC-5: Contract aging schedule defined. CC-7: Contract derivation cost within budget. |

### The Unenforceable Elements Register — Key New Concept

Not every specification element can be structurally enforced. "Reflect possibility" can't be a Guard. "Be genuinely curious" can't be a Revert trigger. These are meaning-space concerns that don't have enforcement-space equivalents.

The Unenforceable Elements Register documents every specification element that:
- Has no contract representation
- Can only be enforced at Regime 1 (Declare — trust the agent)
- Can only be enforced at Regime 2 (Detect — flag after the fact)
- Requires a design decision about whether to accept Regime 1/2 or redesign the specification element to make it Regime 3 enforceable

This register is **honest infrastructure**. It says: "Here's what we can enforce structurally. Here's what we can only detect after the fact. Here's what we can only declare and trust."

---

## Impact on the Three Diamonds

### Before

```
Discovery:  IMAGINE → SPECIFY → TRACE
Structure:   DECOMPOSE → ALLOCATE → STRESS-TEST
Realisation: ARCHITECT → DESIGN → EMPATHIZE → VERIFY
```

### After

```
Discovery:  IMAGINE → SPECIFY → TRACE
Structure:   DECOMPOSE → ALLOCATE → STRESS-TEST
Realisation: ARCHITECT → DESIGN → FORMALIZE → EMPATHIZE → VERIFY
```

The Realisation diamond now has five phases instead of four. But each phase has a sharper purpose:

| Phase | What It Asks |
|---|---|
| ARCHITECT | How do agents compose? |
| DESIGN | What should each agent be? |
| FORMALIZE | What must the system enforce? |
| EMPATHIZE | How does enforcement feel to the human? |
| VERIFY | Does it work? What needs to evolve? |

The progression is: **structure → identity → enforcement → experience → evidence.** Each phase builds on the previous one. You can't formalize what hasn't been designed. You can't design the human experience of enforcement without knowing what's enforced.

---

## Impact on the EVOLVE Cycle

P10 (Validation) spirals back to P0 (Purpose), but the contract formalization creates a specific EVOLVE trigger:

| Signal | What It Means | Where to EVOLVE |
|---|---|---|
| Guard violation rate > 30% | Enforcement isn't working as designed | P8 (Contract Formalization) — is the enforcement regime right? |
| Unenforceable elements accumulating | Specification contains too many meaning-only elements | P7 (Agent Design) — can the specification be redesigned for enforcement? |
| Contract derivation cost exceeding budget | The 10×10 transformation is too expensive | P6 (System Architecture) — can the pipeline simplify the contract? |
| Revert rate > 20% | Output is being rejected too often | P8 (Contract Formalization) — is the revert granularity right? |
| Human override rate > 40% | Humans don't trust the enforcement | P9 (Human Experience) — does the human understand what's enforced? |

---

## One Structural Concern

Adding P8 makes CAWDP an 11-phase process. That's a lot. The Possibility Orientation Principle says P0-P2 should feel expansive. With 11 phases, the structure might feel heavy before you even start.

Two mitigations:

1. **Pattern-first entry hides the structure.** A user selecting "Review Partner" pattern doesn't see 11 phases. They see 4 identity questions, a boundary map, and a finished specification. The contract formalization happens in the back office (Assembler/Contract Drafter), not in the user's experience.

2. **FORMALIZE is a specialist phase, not a universal phase.** Not every agent needs full contract formalization. A simple Extractor running in Shadow mode needs Schema and Event, but not Guard, Revert, or Budget. The 10×10 matrix can be completed at whatever depth is appropriate for the agent's class and autonomy level:

| Agent Class | Typical Contract Depth |
|---|---|
| Extractor (Shadow) | Schema + Event (2 of 10 primitives) |
| Measurer (Supervised) | Schema + Event + Guard (3 of 10) |
| Assessor (Supervised) | Schema + Event + Guard + Principal (4 of 10) |
| Generator (Autonomous) | All 10 primitives + full enforcement regime map |
| Aggregator (Autonomous) | All 10 primitives + full enforcement regime map + cross-agent contract integration |

The phase exists. Its depth varies. Simple agents get shallow formalization. Complex agents get deep formalization. The phase is always present; the work scales with the stakes.

---

**Recommendation: Add P8 Contract Formalization (FORMALIZE) between P7 and P9, making CAWDP an 11-phase process. The phase produces the contract as a distinct stakeholder surface, with its own quality gate, transformation protocol, and the Unenforceable Elements Register as a key new concept.**CAWDP is now an 11-phase process with the specification-to-contract transformation as a first-class phase. The key insight: **formalizing enforcement is a design act, not a translation step**, and it deserves its own cognitive mode (FORMALIZE), its own quality gate, and its own stakeholder surface.

The Unenforceable Elements Register is the most honest artifact this phase produces: *"Here's what we can enforce. Here's what we can only detect. Here's what we can only declare and trust."* That register IS a boundary. And the boundary IS the product.

What would you like to explore next?

---

## Run 16 — Agentic Expert ✓ COMPLETED

*2026-05-19T22:25:40* · `glm-5.1:cloud` · `a3cf89cb...`

### Prompt

Write a full report here (inline) on other changes you would make to improve CAWDP based on the lessons above, and your recommendations on solving the challenges (eg. tool to automate dependency tracing, cognitive load budgeting etc)

### Response

# CAWDP Improvement Report

*Lessons from the meta-application, unresolved challenges, and concrete recommendations*

---

## Executive Summary

The CAWDP v2.1 meta-application (Agent Design Workflow) validated the methodology's internal coherence and produced 11 key findings. This report translates those findings into 19 specific improvements, organized by urgency and feasibility. Each improvement includes the problem, the proposed solution, implementation approach, and connection to existing CAWDP concepts.

---

## Section 1: Methodology Structure Changes

### 1.1 Hardest Boundary Moment as Phase 0 Design Anchor

**The Problem**

The retrospective identified that "the hardest boundary moment reveals identity more than any other question." Currently, the hardest boundary moment is discovered in Phase 7 (Agent Design) retrospective, not specified in Phase 0. For the Mirror agent, it took 3 rounds of iteration because identity was discovered through failure rather than specified upfront.

For the Project Manager, "what should I work on next?" told us more about the agent than Sections 1-6 combined. This question was the stress test that confirmed the agent's identity holds under pressure.

**The Solution**

Add the hardest boundary moment as the **fifth identity question** in Phase 0:

| # | Question | What It Reveals |
|---|---|---|
| Q1 | What IS this agent? | Essence |
| Q2 | What is its stance toward possibility? | Orientation |
| Q3 | What would VIOLATE its identity? | Existential never |
| Q4 | What would make it WRONG? | Failure mode as identity boundary |
| **Q5** | **What's the hardest moment — where pressure would push this agent past its boundary?** | **Stress test that confirms identity** |

This question produces a specific scenario, not an abstract statement. "When the principal says 'just mark it done'" is a scenario. "Maintaining boundaries under pressure" is abstract. The scenario can be tested, simulated, and used as a design anchor for authority boundaries and enforcement regimes.

**Implementation**

- Add Q5 to the Phase 0 identity specification in the Agent Specification Template
- Add Q5 to the Mirror Agent's intake conversation in the Agent Design Workflow
- Use the hardest boundary moment scenario as the first test case in Phase 10 (Validation)
- In Phase 8 (Contract Formalization), the hardest boundary moment should have Regime 3 enforcement wherever possible — if the hardest moment can only be Regime 1 or 2, that's a signal that the enforcement design needs rework

**Connection to existing concepts:** This deepens identity-first design from "4 questions before behavior" to "5 questions before behavior, with the 5th as the design anchor." The hardest boundary moment is the bridge between identity (Phase 0) and enforcement (Phase 8) — it's the specific scenario that both the specification and the contract must address.

---

### 1.2 Per-Dimension Progressive Autonomy as Default

**The Problem**

The retrospective found inconsistent application of per-dimension progressive autonomy. The Project Manager has 6 dimensions (access control NEVER fully autonomous). The Code Reviewer has 6 dimensions (security NEVER fully autonomous). But other agents have a single progression as if all dimensions earn trust at the same rate.

Every agent has at least one dimension where full autonomy is inappropriate. Making per-dimension a special case creates inconsistency and misses the core insight: trust is dimensional, not monolithic.

**The Solution**

Make per-dimension progressive autonomy the **default question** in Phase 7 (Agent Design), not an optional extension.

**Revised template structure:**

| Section | Current | Revised |
|---|---|---|
| Progressive Autonomy | Single 4-level progression | Per-dimension table with 3-6 dimensions per agent |

**Default dimensions by agent class:**

| Agent Class | Minimum Dimensions | Example |
|---|---|---|
| Extractor | 2 | Data access, Output format |
| Measurer | 3 | Measurement method, Threshold application, Reporting |
| Assessor | 3 | Evidence evaluation, Recommendation strength, Final judgment |
| Generator | 4 | Content creation, Safety assessment, Style, Domain expertise |
| Aggregator | 3 | Assembly, Addition detection, Cross-agent coordination |

Every dimension has:
- A trust ceiling (some dimensions say "NEVER fully autonomous")
- Promotion criteria specific to that dimension
- Override rate thresholds for promotion/demotion
- A specification aging cadence for re-evaluation

**Implementation**

- Add per-dimension progressive autonomy table to the Agent Specification Template as a required section
- Add a quality gate check: "Does every agent have at least one dimension where full autonomy is inappropriate?"
- Add the per-dimension table to the Mirror Agent's specification output in the Agent Design Workflow
- For simple agents (Extractor, Measurer), the table may only have 2-3 rows. The point is that the question is always asked, not that the answer is always complex.

**Connection to existing concepts:** This operationalizes CC-6 (System Empowerment Index) at the dimension level. Each dimension has its own empowerment trajectory. The Trust Ledger (Milestone 4) tracks per-dimension metrics, making this measurable.

---

### 1.3 Null-State Output as Universal Hard Constraint

**The Problem**

"Silence ≠ absence" was identified for monitoring/signal agents (the Watchman). But the principle generalizes to every agent class. If an Extractor finds no results, it should produce "No results found" with epistemic metadata, not return nothing. If a Measurer has insufficient data, it should produce "Insufficient data for measurement" with what it needs, not skip the output.

The Project Manager retrospective identified null-state output as a universal requirement, not a special case.

**The Solution**

Add null-state output as a **universal hard constraint** in the Agent Specification Template:

| Agent Class | Null-State Output | Format |
|---|---|---|
| Extractor | "No results found" + search parameters + scope covered | Typed object |
| Measurer | "Insufficient data" + what's missing + minimum required | Typed object |
| Assessor | "Unable to assess" + reason + what would enable assessment | Typed object |
| Generator | "Cannot generate" + reason + alternatives or de-escalation | Typed object |
| Aggregator | "Nothing to assemble" + what inputs are missing | Typed object |

Every null-state output carries epistemic metadata (confidence: 0, provenance: self-report, limitations: complete). The system can distinguish between "agent ran and found nothing" and "agent didn't run."

**Implementation**

- Add null-state output requirement to the Agent Specification Template as a hard constraint
- Add null-state output schemas to the Pattern Library pre-fills
- Add a quality gate check: "Does every agent produce output in all states including null state?"
- In Phase 8 (Contract Formalization), the Event primitive should include null-state events as a contract requirement

**Connection to existing concepts:** This is "silence ≠ absence" generalized from monitoring agents to all agents. It connects to CC-3 (Epistemic Metadata) — even null states carry metadata. It connects to the Event primitive — null-state events enable the system to distinguish silence from absence at the audit layer.

---

### 1.4 Indirect Data Harm Alongside Direct Output Harm

**The Problem**

The harm assessment currently focuses on direct output harm — can the agent's output cause harm if accepted? (SuggestionRisk schema, DO_NOT_SUGGEST mechanism). But the Project Manager identified a different category: **indirect data harm** — wrong status, missing dependency, incorrect authorization that cascades into wrong decisions downstream.

The Project Manager can't produce harmful content (it manages structure, not content). But it can store wrong data that causes wrong decisions. These require different design responses.

**The Solution**

Add a two-category harm assessment to the Agent Specification Template:

| Category | Mechanism | Example | Design Response |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Direct output harm** | Agent produces content that causes harm if accepted | Code reviewer suggests vulnerable code | SuggestionRisk schema, DO_NOT_SUGGEST, self-evaluation mode |
| **Indirect data harm** | Agent stores/retrieves wrong data causing wrong decisions downstream | Project Manager marks task complete when it isn't | Data integrity enforcement, CC-4 at database layer, verification spot-checks |

**Harm assessment flow in the template:**

```
1. Can this agent's output cause direct harm if accepted?
   → YES: Apply SuggestionRisk schema + DO_NOT_SUGGEST mechanism
   → NO: Continue to question 2

2. Can wrong data stored by this agent cause indirect harm through downstream decisions?
   → YES: Apply data integrity enforcement (CC-4 Regime 3, verification spot-checks)
   → NO: Document as low-harm agent
```

**Implementation**

- Split the harm assessment section in the Agent Specification Template into two questions
- Add indirect data harm design patterns to the Pattern Library
- For data-managing agents (Aggregators, registries), the default harm assumption shifts from "direct output harm" to "indirect data harm"
- Add data integrity verification to the Trust Ledger as a standard metric

**Connection to existing concepts:** The Project Manager's private schema pattern (CC-4 at database layer) IS the design response for indirect data harm. The SuggestionRisk schema is the design response for direct output harm. These are two different Regime 3 enforcement mechanisms for two different harm categories.

---

### 1.5 Enforcement Regime Specification for Every Boundary

**The Problem**

The retrospective identified that boundaries are declared but enforcement regimes aren't specified. The Agent Specification Template says "never decides for the human" but doesn't say whether this is Regime 1 (declare — trust the agent), Regime 2 (detect — flag after the fact), or Regime 3 (prevent — system enforces structurally).

Without enforcement regime specification, every boundary is effectively Regime 1. The contract formalization (P8) converts declared boundaries into enforced boundaries, but the specification should identify the target regime upfront so the Contract Drafter knows what to aim for.

**The Solution**

Add enforcement regime as a required field for every authority boundary in the Agent Specification Template:

| Boundary | Current | Revised |
|---|---|---|
| "Never decides for the human" | Declared | Declared (target: Regime 3 — system prevents via Guard) |
| "Never fabricates evidence" | Declared | Declared (target: Regime 2 — system detects via output audit) |
| "Always includes confidence score" | Declared | Declared (target: Regime 3 — system enforces via Schema validation) |

Each boundary has:
- Current regime (always "Declared" in the specification phase)
- Target regime (what P8 Contract Formalization should aim for)
- Justification (why this regime, why not higher)
- Implementation note (what mechanism would achieve the target regime)

**Implementation**

- Add enforcement regime fields to the Agent Specification Template boundaries section
- Add target regime justification as a quality gate check in P7 and P8
- The Unenforceable Elements Register (P8) documents boundaries where the target regime can't be achieved — these are honest infrastructure gaps
- Pattern Library pre-fills include default enforcement regime recommendations per agent class

**Connection to existing concepts:** This bridges P7 (Agent Design — declare boundaries) and P8 (Contract Formalization — enforce boundaries). The target regime tells the Contract Drafter what to aim for. The Unenforceable Elements Register documents where the target can't be met. This is the "speed limit sign vs speed limiter" insight applied systematically.

---

## Section 2: Process and Tooling Improvements

### 2.1 Dependency Tracing Tool for Phase 2 Backcasting

**The Problem**

Phase 2 (Backcasting) requires tracing dependency chains backward from 29 outputs to approximately 75 input requirements. In the meta-application, we derived 18 explicitly and documented the methodology for the rest. The mechanical complexity of full backcasting is significant — each output has 2-5 input requirements, and each input requirement has a criticality level and satisfaction mode.

Doing this manually for a 29-output workflow took substantial effort. For larger workflows (the assignment marking pipeline had 36 subtasks), full backcasting without tooling is impractical.

**The Solution**

Build a **Backcasting Engine** that automates dependency tracing from Phase 1 outputs:

**Core Algorithm:**

```
For each output O in Phase 1:
  1. Identify direct dependencies (what O needs to exist)
  2. For each dependency D:
     a. If D is an external input: create InputRequirement(type=external, criticality=infer, satisfaction=direct)
     b. If D is another output O': create InputRequirement(type=internal, source=O')
     c. Recurse on O' until all dependencies trace to external inputs or circular dependencies detected
  3. Assign criticality:
     - Final deliverable dependencies → CRITICAL
     - Intermediate output dependencies → inferred from downstream criticality
     - Quality gate dependencies → HIGH
     - Supporting document dependencies → MEDIUM
  4. Assign satisfaction mode:
     - External inputs → DIRECT (must be provided)
     - Internal outputs → DERIVED (produced by earlier phase)
     - Partial dependencies → PARTIAL (produced by earlier phase, refined later)
```

**Implementation Architecture:**

```
backcasting_engine/
  ├── models.py          # InputRequirement, OutputSpec, DependencyChain Pydantic models
  ├── engine.py          # Core backcasting algorithm
  ├── criticality.py     # Criticality inference rules
  ├── visualizer.py      # Dependency graph visualization
  ├── gap_detector.py     # Detects missing inputs, circular dependencies, orphan outputs
  └── cli.py             # Command-line interface
```

**Gap Detection (the highest-value feature):**

| Gap Type | Detection Method | What It Reveals |
|---|---|---|
| Missing input | Output has dependency with no source | Something needs to exist that nobody produces |
| Circular dependency | Cycle detected in dependency graph | Two outputs depend on each other — phase ordering problem |
| Orphan output | Output has no dependents | Something is produced that nobody needs |
| Critical path gap | Critical dependency has no satisfaction mode | The pipeline can't produce its final deliverable |
| Quality gate gap | Gate requires output not in dependency chain | A quality gate references something the pipeline doesn't produce |

**Integration with CAWDP Design Studio:**

The Backcasting Engine should be integrated into the Design Studio as the P2 workspace. Users specify outputs in P1, the engine traces dependencies in P2, and the gap detector surfaces issues before the user moves to P3.

**Connection to existing concepts:** This is the TRACE cognitive mode made operational. The engine does the mechanical work of dependency tracing so the human can focus on the design decisions (criticality assignment, satisfaction mode selection, gap resolution). This is "agent prepares judgment, human makes judgment" applied to the methodology itself.

---

### 2.2 Cognitive Load Budget Calculator

**The Problem**

The 5-decisions-per-session budget is based on research-informed estimates, not empirical data. Decision density varies by user (Sarah Chen has 30 minutes; Daniel Kovacs might have 2 hours), by domain (commercial law decisions carry more weight than marketing copy decisions), and by session (the first Boundary Map session is more cognitively demanding than a Trust Ledger review).

The current cognitive load budget is a fixed number. It should be adaptive.

**The Solution**

Build a **Cognitive Load Budget Calculator** that produces per-session, per-user, per-domain budgets:

**Budget Formula:**

```
decisions_per_session = base_capacity
  × domain_weight
  × user_experience_modifier
  × session_sequence_modifier
  × decision_complexity_modifier
```

**Component Definitions:**

| Factor | How It's Set | Example Values |
|---|---|---|
| Base capacity | Research baseline (5 decisions/session for unfamiliar domain) | 3 (novice) → 5 (standard) → 8 (expert) |
| Domain weight | Stakes classification from P0 | 0.7 (high-stakes legal) → 1.0 (standard) → 1.3 (low-stakes operational) |
